TOM school or to church, or call any being master or obey anybody; he could
TOM go fishing or swimming when and where he chose, and stay as long as it
TOM suited him; nobody forbade him to fight; he could sit up as late as he
TOM pleased; he was always the first boy that went barefoot in the spring
TOM and the last to resume leather in the fall; he never had to wash, nor
TOM put on clean clothes; he could swear wonderfully. In a word, everything
TOM that goes to make life precious that boy had. So thought every harassed,
TOM hampered, respectable boy in St. Petersburg.
TOM 
TOM Tom hailed the romantic outcast:
TOM 
TOM ``Hello, Huckleberry!''
TOM 
TOM ``Hello yourself, and see how you like it.''
TOM 
TOM ``What's that you got?''
TOM 
TOM ``Dead cat.''
TOM 
TOM ``Lemme see him, Huck. My, he's pretty stiff. Where'd you get him ?''
TOM 
TOM ``Bought him off'n a boy.''
TOM 
TOM ``What did you give?''
TOM 
TOM ``I give a blue ticket and a bladder that I got at the
TOM slaughter-house.''
TOM 
TOM ``Where'd you get the blue ticket?''
TOM 
TOM ``Bought it off'n Ben Rogers two weeks ago for a hoop-stick.''
TOM 
TOM ``Say -- what is dead cats good for, Huck?''
TOM 
TOM ``Good for? Cure warts with.''
TOM 
TOM ``No! Is that so? I know something that's better.''
TOM 
TOM ``I bet you don't. What is it?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, spunk-water.''
TOM 
TOM ``Spunk-water! I wouldn't give a dern for spunk-water.''
TOM 
TOM ``You wouldn't, wouldn't you? D'you ever try it?''
TOM 
TOM ``No, I hain't. But Bob Tanner did.''
TOM 
TOM ``Who told you so!''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, he told Jeff Thatcher, and Jeff told Johnny Baker, and Johnny
TOM told Jim Hollis, and Jim told Ben Rogers, and Ben told a nigger, and the
TOM nigger told me. There now!''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, what of it? They'll all lie. Leastways all but the nigger. I
TOM don't know HIM. But I never see a nigger that WOULDN'T lie. Shucks! Now
TOM you tell me how Bob Tanner done it, Huck.''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, he took and dipped his hand in a rotten stump where the
TOM rain-water was.''
TOM 
TOM ``In the daytime?''
TOM 
TOM ``Certainly.''
TOM 
TOM ``With his face to the stump?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes. Least I reckon so.''
TOM 
TOM ``Did he say anything?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't reckon he did. I don't know.''
TOM 
TOM ``Aha! Talk about trying to cure warts with spunk-water such a blame
TOM fool way as that! Why, that ain't a-going to do any good. You got to go
TOM all by yourself, to the middle of the woods, where you know there's a
TOM spunk-water stump, and just as it's midnight you back up against the
TOM stump and jam your hand in and say:
TOM 
TOM 'Barley-corn, barley-corn, injun-meal shorts, Spunk-water, spunk-water,
TOM swaller these warts,'
TOM 
TOM and then walk away quick, eleven steps, with your eyes shut, and then
TOM turn around three times and walk home without speaking to anybody.
TOM Because if you speak the charm's busted.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, that sounds like a good way; but that ain't the way Bob Tanner
TOM done.''
TOM 
TOM ``No, sir, you can bet he didn't, becuz he's the wartiest boy in this
TOM town; and he wouldn't have a wart on him if he'd knowed how to work
TOM spunk-water. I've took off thousands of warts off of my hands that way,
TOM Huck. I play with frogs so much that I've always got considerable many
TOM warts. Sometimes I take 'em off with a bean.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, bean's good. I've done that.''
TOM 
TOM ``Have you? What's your way?''
TOM 
TOM ``You take and split the bean, and cut the wart so as to get some blood,
TOM and then you put the blood on one piece of the bean and take and dig a
TOM hole and bury it 'bout midnight at the crossroads in the dark of the
TOM moon, and then you burn up the rest of the bean. You see that piece
TOM that's got the blood on it will keep drawing and drawing, trying to
TOM fetch the other piece to it, and so that helps the blood to draw the
TOM wart, and pretty soon off she comes.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, that's it, Huck -- that's it; though when you're burying it if
TOM you say 'Down bean; off wart; come no more to bother me!' it's better.
TOM That's the way Joe Harper does, and he's been nearly to Coonville and
TOM most everywheres. But say -- how do you cure 'em with dead cats?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, you take your cat and go and get in the graveyard 'long about
TOM midnight when somebody that was wicked has been buried; and when it's
TOM midnight a devil will come, or maybe two or three, but you can't see
TOM 'em, you can only hear something like the wind, or maybe hear 'em talk;
TOM and when they're taking that feller away, you heave your cat after 'em
TOM and say, 'Devil follow corpse, cat follow devil, warts follow cat, I'm
TOM done with ye!' That'll fetch ANY wart.''
TOM 
TOM ``Sounds right. D'you ever try it, Huck?''
TOM 
TOM ``No, but old Mother Hopkins told me.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I reckon it's so, then. Becuz they say she's a witch.''
TOM 
TOM ``Say! Why, Tom, I KNOW she is. She witched pap. Pap says so his own
TOM self. He come along one day, and he see she was a-witching him, so he
TOM took up a rock, and if she hadn't dodged, he'd a got her. Well, that
TOM very night he rolled off'n a shed wher' he was a layin drunk, and broke
TOM his arm.''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, that's awful. How did he know she was a-witching him?''
TOM 
TOM ``Lord, pap can tell, easy. Pap says when they keep looking at you right
TOM stiddy, they're a-witching you. Specially if they mumble. Becuz when
TOM they mumble they're saying the Lord's Prayer backards.''
TOM 
TOM ``Say, Hucky, when you going to try the cat?''
TOM 
TOM ``To-night. I reckon they'll come after old Hoss Williams to-night.''
TOM 
TOM ``But they buried him Saturday. Didn't they get him Saturday night?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, how you talk! How could their charms work till midnight? -- and
TOM THEN it's Sunday. Devils don't slosh around much of a Sunday, I don't
TOM reckon.''
TOM 
TOM ``I never thought of that. That's so. Lemme go with you?''
TOM 
TOM ``Of course -- if you ain't afeard.''
TOM 
TOM ``Afeard! 'Tain't likely. Will you meow?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes -- and you meow back, if you get a chance. Last time, you kep' me
TOM a-meowing around till old Hays went to throwing rocks at me and says
TOM 'Dern that cat!' and so I hove a brick through his window -- but don't
TOM you tell.''
TOM 
TOM ``I won't. I couldn't meow that night, becuz auntie was watching me, but
TOM I'll meow this time. Say -- what's that?''
TOM 
TOM ``Nothing but a tick.''
TOM 
TOM ``Where'd you get him?''
TOM 
TOM ``Out in the woods.''
TOM 
TOM ``What'll you take for him?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't know. I don't want to sell him.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right. It's a mighty small tick, anyway.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, anybody can run a tick down that don't belong to them. I'm
TOM satisfied with it. It's a good enough tick for me.''
TOM 
TOM ``Sho, there's ticks a plenty. I could have a thousand of 'em if I
TOM wanted to.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, why don't you? Becuz you know mighty well you can't. This is a
TOM pretty early tick, I reckon. It's the first one I've seen this year.''
TOM 
TOM ``Say, Huck -- I'll give you my tooth for him.''
TOM 
TOM ``Less see it.''
TOM 
TOM Tom got out a bit of paper and carefully unrolled it. Huckleberry viewed
TOM it wistfully. The temptation was very strong. At last he said:
TOM 
TOM ``Is it genuwyne?''
TOM 
TOM Tom lifted his lip and showed the vacancy.
TOM 
TOM ``Well, all right,'' said Huckleberry, ``it's a trade.''
TOM 
TOM Tom enclosed the tick in the percussion-cap box that had lately been the
TOM pinchbug's prison, and the boys separated, each feeling wealthier than
TOM before.
TOM 
TOM When Tom reached the little isolated frame school-house, he strode in
TOM briskly, with the manner of one who had come with all honest speed. He
TOM hung his hat on a peg and flung himself into his seat with business-like
TOM alacrity. The master, throned on high in his great splint-bottom
TOM arm-chair, was dozing, lulled by the drowsy hum of study. The
TOM interruption roused him.
TOM 
TOM ``Thomas Sawyer!''
TOM 
TOM Tom knew that when his name was pronounced in full, it meant trouble.
TOM 
TOM ``Sir!''
TOM 
TOM ``Come up here. Now, sir, why are you late again, as usual?''
TOM 
TOM Tom was about to take refuge in a lie, when he saw two long tails of
TOM yellow hair hanging down a back that he recognized by the electric
TOM sympathy of love; and by that form was THE ONLY VACANT PLACE on the
TOM girls' side of the school-house. He instantly said:
TOM 
TOM ``I STOPPED TO TALK WITH HUCKLEBERRY FINN!''
TOM 
TOM The master's pulse stood still, and he stared helplessly. The buzz of
TOM study ceased. The pupils wondered if this foolhardy boy had lost his
TOM mind. The master said:
TOM 
TOM ``You -- you did what?''
TOM 
TOM ``Stopped to talk with Huckleberry Finn.''
TOM 
TOM There was no mistaking the words.
TOM 
TOM ``Thomas Sawyer, this is the most astounding confession I have ever
TOM listened to. No mere ferule will answer for this offence. Take off your
TOM jacket.''
TOM 
TOM The master's arm performed until it was tired and the stock of switches
TOM notably diminished. Then the order followed:
TOM 
TOM ``Now, sir, go and sit with the girls! And let this be a warning to
TOM you.''
TOM 
TOM The titter that rippled around the room appeared to abash the boy, but
TOM in reality that result was caused rather more by his worshipful awe of
TOM his unknown idol and the dread pleasure that lay in his high good
TOM fortune. He sat down upon the end of the pine bench and the girl hitched
TOM herself away from him with a toss of her head. Nudges and winks and
TOM whispers traversed the room, but Tom sat still, with his arms upon the
TOM long, low desk before him, and seemed to study his book.
TOM 
TOM By and by attention ceased from him, and the accustomed school murmur
TOM rose upon the dull air once more. Presently the boy began to steal
TOM furtive glances at the girl. She observed it, ``made a mouth'' at him
TOM and gave him the back of her head for the space of a minute. When she
TOM cautiously faced around again, a peach lay before her. She thrust it
TOM away. Tom gently put it back. She thrust it away again, but with less
TOM animosity. Tom patiently returned it to its place. Then she let it
TOM remain. Tom scrawled on his slate, ``Please take it -- I got more.'' The
TOM girl glanced at the words, but made no sign. Now the boy began to draw
TOM something on the slate, hiding his work with his left hand. For a time
TOM the girl refused to notice; but her human curiosity presently began to
TOM manifest itself by hardly perceptible signs. The boy worked on,
TOM apparently unconscious. The girl made a sort of non-committal attempt to
TOM see, but the boy did not betray that he was aware of it. At last she
TOM gave in and hesitatingly whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``Let me see it.''
TOM 
TOM Tom partly uncovered a dismal caricature of a house with two gable ends
TOM to it and a corkscrew of smoke issuing from the chimney. Then the girl's
TOM interest began to fasten itself upon the work and she forgot everything
TOM else. When it was finished, she gazed a moment, then whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``It's nice -- make a man.''
TOM 
TOM The artist erected a man in the front yard, that resembled a derrick. He
TOM could have stepped over the house; but the girl was not hypercritical;
TOM she was satisfied with the monster, and whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``It's a beautiful man -- now make me coming along.''
TOM 
TOM Tom drew an hour-glass with a full moon and straw limbs to it and armed
TOM the spreading fingers with a portentous fan. The girl said:
TOM 
TOM ``It's ever so nice -- I wish I could draw.''
TOM 
TOM ``It's easy,'' whispered Tom, ``I'll learn you.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, will you? When?''
TOM 
TOM ``At noon. Do you go home to dinner?''
TOM 
TOM ``I'll stay if you will.''
TOM 
TOM ``Good -- that's a whack. What's your name?''
TOM 
TOM ``Becky Thatcher. What's yours? Oh, I know. It's Thomas Sawyer.''
TOM 
TOM ``That's the name they lick me by. I'm Tom when I'm good. You call me
TOM Tom, will you?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes.''
TOM 
TOM Now Tom began to scrawl something on the slate, hiding the words from
TOM the girl. But she was not backward this time. She begged to see. Tom
TOM said:
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, it ain't anything.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes it is.''
TOM 
TOM ``No it ain't. You don't want to see.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes I do, indeed I do. Please let me.''
TOM 
TOM ``You'll tell.''
TOM 
TOM ``No I won't -- deed and deed and double deed won't.''
TOM 
TOM ``You won't tell anybody at all? Ever, as long as you live?''
TOM 
TOM ``No, I won't ever tell ANYbody. Now let me.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, YOU don't want to see!''
TOM 
TOM ``Now that you treat me so, I WILL see.'' And she put her small hand
TOM upon his and a little scuffle ensued, Tom pretending to resist in
TOM earnest but letting his hand slip by degrees till these words were
TOM revealed: ``I LOVE YOU.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, you bad thing!'' And she hit his hand a smart rap, but reddened
TOM and looked pleased, nevertheless.
TOM 
TOM Just at this juncture the boy felt a slow, fateful grip closing on his
TOM ear, and a steady lifting impulse. In that vise he was borne across the
TOM house and deposited in his own seat, under a peppering fire of giggles
TOM from the whole school. Then the master stood over him during a few awful
TOM moments, and finally moved away to his throne without saying a word. But
TOM although Tom's ear tingled, his heart was jubilant.
TOM 
TOM As the school quieted down Tom made an honest effort to study, but the
TOM turmoil within him was too great. In turn he took his place in the
TOM reading class and made a botch of it; then in the geography class and
TOM turned lakes into mountains, mountains into rivers, and rivers into
TOM continents, till chaos was come again; then in the spelling class, and
TOM got ``turned down,'' by a succession of mere baby words, till he brought
TOM up at the foot and yielded up the pewter medal which he had worn with
TOM ostentation for months.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter VII
TOM 
TOM THE harder Tom tried to fasten his mind on his book, the more his ideas
TOM wandered. So at last, with a sigh and a yawn, he gave it up. It seemed
TOM to him that the noon recess would never come. The air was utterly dead.
TOM There was not a breath stirring. It was the sleepiest of sleepy days.
TOM The drowsing murmur of the five and twenty studying scholars soothed the
TOM soul like the spell that is in the murmur of bees. Away off in the
TOM flaming sunshine, Cardiff Hill lifted its soft green sides through a
TOM shimmering veil of heat, tinted with the purple of distance; a few birds
TOM floated on lazy wing high in the air; no other living thing was visible
TOM but some cows, and they were asleep. Tom's heart ached to be free, or
TOM else to have something of interest to do to pass the dreary time. His
TOM hand wandered into his pocket and his face lit up with a glow of
TOM gratitude that was prayer, though he did not know it. Then furtively the
TOM percussion-cap box came out. He released the tick and put him on the
TOM long flat desk. The creature probably glowed with a gratitude that
TOM amounted to prayer, too, at this moment, but it was premature: for when
TOM he started thankfully to travel off, Tom turned him aside with a pin and
TOM made him take a new direction.
TOM 
TOM Tom's bosom friend sat next him, suffering just as Tom had been, and now
TOM he was deeply and gratefully interested in this entertainment in an
TOM instant. This bosom friend was Joe Harper. The two boys were sworn
TOM friends all the week, and embattled enemies on Saturdays. Joe took a pin
TOM out of his lapel and began to assist in exercising the prisoner. The
TOM sport grew in interest momently. Soon Tom said that they were
TOM interfering with each other, and neither getting the fullest benefit of
TOM the tick. So he put Joe's slate on the desk and drew a line down the
TOM middle of it from top to bottom.
TOM 
TOM ``Now,'' said he, ``as long as he is on your side you can stir him up
TOM and I'll let him alone; but if you let him get away and get on my side,
TOM you're to leave him alone as long as I can keep him from crossing
TOM over.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right, go ahead; start him up.''
TOM 
TOM The tick escaped from Tom, presently, and crossed the equator. Joe
TOM harassed him awhile, and then he got away and crossed back again. This
TOM change of base occurred often. While one boy was worrying the tick with
TOM absorbing interest, the other would look on with interest as strong, the
TOM two heads bowed together over the slate, and the two souls dead to all
TOM things else. At last luck seemed to settle and abide with Joe. The tick
TOM tried this, that, and the other course, and got as excited and as
TOM anxious as the boys themselves, but time and again just as he would have
TOM victory in his very grasp, so to speak, and Tom's fingers would be
TOM twitching to begin, Joe's pin would deftly head him off, and keep
TOM possession. At last Tom could stand it no longer. The temptation was too
TOM strong. So he reached out and lent a hand with his pin. Joe was angry in
TOM a moment. Said he:
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, you let him alone.''
TOM 
TOM ``I only just want to stir him up a little, Joe.''
TOM 
TOM ``No, sir, it ain't fair; you just let him alone.''
TOM 
TOM ``Blame it, I ain't going to stir him much.''
TOM 
TOM ``Let him alone, I tell you.''
TOM 
TOM ``I won't!''
TOM 
TOM ``You shall -- he's on my side of the line.''
TOM 
TOM ``Look here, Joe Harper, whose is that tick?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't care whose tick he is -- he's on my side of the line, and you
TOM sha'n't touch him.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I'll just bet I will, though. He's my tick and I'll do what I
TOM blame please with him, or die!''
TOM 
TOM A tremendous whack came down on Tom's shoulders, and its duplicate on
TOM Joe's; and for the space of two minutes the dust continued to fly from
TOM the two jackets and the whole school to enjoy it. The boys had been too
TOM absorbed to notice the hush that had stolen upon the school awhile
TOM before when the master came tiptoeing down the room and stood over them.
TOM He had contemplated a good part of the performance before he contributed
TOM his bit of variety to it.
TOM 
TOM When school broke up at noon, Tom flew to Becky Thatcher, and whispered
TOM in her ear:
TOM 
TOM ``Put on your bonnet and let on you're going home; and when you get to
TOM the corner, give the rest of 'em the slip, and turn down through the
TOM lane and come back. I'll go the other way and come it over 'em the same
TOM way.''
TOM 
TOM So the one went off with one group of scholars, and the other with
TOM another. In a little while the two met at the bottom of the lane, and
TOM when they reached the school they had it all to themselves. Then they
TOM sat together, with a slate before them, and Tom gave Becky the pencil
TOM and held her hand in his, guiding it, and so created another surprising
TOM house. When the interest in art began to wane, the two fell to talking.
TOM Tom was swimming in bliss. He said:
TOM 
TOM ``Do you love rats?''
TOM 
TOM ``No! I hate them!''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I do, too -- LIVE ones. But I mean dead ones, to swing round
TOM your head with a string.''
TOM 
TOM ``No, I don't care for rats much, anyway. What I like is chewing-gum.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, I should say so! I wish I had some now.''
TOM 
TOM ``Do you? I've got some. I'll let you chew it awhile, but you must give
TOM it back to me.''
TOM 
TOM That was agreeable, so they chewed it turn about, and dangled their legs
TOM against the bench in excess of contentment.
TOM 
TOM ``Was you ever at a circus?'' said Tom.
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, and my pa's going to take me again some time, if I'm good.''
TOM 
TOM ``I been to the circus three or four times -- lots of times. Church
TOM ain't shucks to a circus. There's things going on at a circus all the
TOM time. I'm going to be a clown in a circus when I grow up.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, are you! That will be nice. They're so lovely, all spotted up.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, that's so. And they get slathers of money -- most a dollar a day,
TOM Ben Rogers says. Say, Becky, was you ever engaged?''
TOM 
TOM ``What's that?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, engaged to be married.''
TOM 
TOM ``No.''
TOM 
TOM ``Would you like to?''
TOM 
TOM ``I reckon so. I don't know. What is it like?''
TOM 
TOM ``Like? Why it ain't like anything. You only just tell a boy you won't
TOM ever have anybody but him, ever ever ever, and then you kiss and that's
TOM all. Anybody can do it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Kiss? What do you kiss for?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, that, you know, is to -- well, they always do that.''
TOM 
TOM ``Everybody?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, yes, everybody that's in love with each other. Do you remember
TOM what I wrote on the slate?''
TOM 
TOM ``Ye -- yes.''
TOM 
TOM ``What was it?''
TOM 
TOM ``I sha'n't tell you.''
TOM 
TOM ``Shall I tell YOU?''
TOM 
TOM ``Ye -- yes -- but some other time.''
TOM 
TOM ``No, now.''
TOM 
TOM ``No, not now -- to-morrow.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, no, NOW. Please, Becky -- I'll whisper it, I'll whisper it ever so
TOM easy.''
TOM 
TOM Becky hesitating, Tom took silence for consent, and passed his arm about
TOM her waist and whispered the tale ever so softly, with his mouth close to
TOM her ear. And then he added:
TOM 
TOM ``Now you whisper it to me -- just the same.''
TOM 
TOM She resisted, for a while, and then said:
TOM 
TOM ``You turn your face away so you can't see, and then I will. But you
TOM mustn't ever tell anybody -- WILL you, Tom? Now you won't, WILL you?''
TOM 
TOM ``No, indeed, indeed I won't. Now, Becky.''
TOM 
TOM He turned his face away. She bent timidly around till her breath stirred
TOM his curls and whispered, ``I -- love -- you!''
TOM 
TOM Then she sprang away and ran around and around the desks and benches,
TOM with Tom after her, and took refuge in a corner at last, with her little
TOM white apron to her face. Tom clasped her about her neck and pleaded:
TOM 
TOM ``Now, Becky, it's all done -- all over but the kiss. Don't you be
TOM afraid of that -- it ain't anything at all. Please, Becky.'' And he
TOM tugged at her apron and the hands.
TOM 
TOM By and by she gave up, and let her hands drop; her face, all glowing
TOM with the struggle, came up and submitted. Tom kissed the red lips and
TOM said:
TOM 
TOM ``Now it's all done, Becky. And always after this, you know, you ain't
TOM ever to love anybody but me, and you ain't ever to marry anybody but me,
TOM ever never and forever. Will you?''
TOM 
TOM ``No, I'll never love anybody but you, Tom, and I'll never marry anybody
TOM but you -- and you ain't to ever marry anybody but me, either.''
TOM 
TOM ``Certainly. Of course. That's PART of it. And always coming to school
TOM or when we're going home, you're to walk with me, when there ain't
TOM anybody looking -- and you choose me and I choose you at parties,
TOM because that's the way you do when you're engaged.''
TOM 
TOM ``It's so nice. I never heard of it before.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, it's ever so gay! Why, me and Amy Lawrence --''
TOM 
TOM The big eyes told Tom his blunder and he stopped, confused.
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, Tom! Then I ain't the first you've ever been engaged to!''
TOM 
TOM The child began to cry. Tom said:
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, don't cry, Becky, I don't care for her any more.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, you do, Tom -- you know you do.''
TOM 
TOM Tom tried to put his arm about her neck, but she pushed him away and
TOM turned her face to the wall, and went on crying. Tom tried again, with
TOM soothing words in his mouth, and was repulsed again. Then his pride was
TOM up, and he strode away and went outside. He stood about, restless and
TOM uneasy, for a while, glancing at the door, every now and then, hoping
TOM she would repent and come to find him. But she did not. Then he began to
TOM feel badly and fear that he was in the wrong. It was a hard struggle
TOM with him to make new advances, now, but he nerved himself to it and
TOM entered. She was still standing back there in the corner, sobbing, with
TOM her face to the wall. Tom's heart smote him. He went to her and stood a
TOM moment, not knowing exactly how to proceed. Then he said hesitatingly:
TOM 
TOM ``Becky, I -- I don't care for anybody but you.''
TOM 
TOM No reply -- but sobs.
TOM 
TOM ``Becky'' -- pleadingly. ``Becky, won't you say something?''
TOM 
TOM More sobs.
TOM 
TOM Tom got out his chiefest jewel, a brass knob from the top of an andiron,
TOM and passed it around her so that she could see it, and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Please, Becky, won't you take it?''
TOM 
TOM She struck it to the floor. Then Tom marched out of the house and over
TOM the hills and far away, to return to school no more that day. Presently
TOM Becky began to suspect. She ran to the door; he was not in sight; she
TOM flew around to the play-yard; he was not there. Then she called:
TOM 
TOM ``Tom! Come back, Tom!''
TOM 
TOM She listened intently, but there was no answer. She had no companions
TOM but silence and loneliness. So she sat down to cry again and upbraid
TOM herself; and by this time the scholars began to gather again, and she
TOM had to hide her griefs and still her broken heart and take up the cross
TOM of a long, dreary, aching afternoon, with none among the strangers about
TOM her to exchange sorrows with.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter VIII
TOM 
TOM TOM dodged hither and thither through lanes until he was well out of the
TOM track of returning scholars, and then fell into a moody jog. He crossed
TOM a small ``branch'' two or three times, because of a prevailing juvenile
TOM superstition that to cross water baffled pursuit. Half an hour later he
TOM was disappearing behind the Douglas mansion on the summit of Cardiff
TOM Hill, and the school-house was hardly distinguishable away off in the
TOM valley behind him. He entered a dense wood, picked his pathless way to
TOM the centre of it, and sat down on a mossy spot under a spreading oak.
TOM There was not even a zephyr stirring; the dead noonday heat had even
TOM stilled the songs of the birds; nature lay in a trance that was broken
TOM by no sound but the occasional far-off hammering of a wood-pecker, and
TOM this seemed to render the pervading silence and sense of loneliness the
TOM more profound. The boy's soul was steeped in melancholy; his feelings
TOM were in happy accord with his surroundings. He sat long with his elbows
TOM on his knees and his chin in his hands, meditating. It seemed to him
TOM that life was but a trouble, at best, and he more than half envied Jimmy
TOM Hodges, so lately released; it must be very peaceful, he thought, to lie
TOM and slumber and dream forever and ever, with the wind whispering through
TOM the trees and caressing the grass and the flowers over the grave, and
TOM nothing to bother and grieve about, ever any more. If he only had a
TOM clean Sunday-school record he could be willing to go, and be done with
TOM it all. Now as to this girl. What had he done? Nothing. He had meant the
TOM best in the world, and been treated like a dog -- like a very dog. She
TOM would be sorry some day -- maybe when it was too late. Ah, if he could
TOM only die TEMPORARILY!
TOM 
TOM But the elastic heart of youth cannot be compressed into one constrained
TOM shape long at a time. Tom presently began to drift insensibly back into
TOM the concerns of this life again. What if he turned his back, now, and
TOM disappeared mysteriously? What if he went away -- ever so far away, into
TOM unknown countries beyond the seas -- and never came back any more! How
TOM would she feel then! The idea of being a clown recurred to him now, only
TOM to fill him with disgust. For frivolity and jokes and spotted tights
TOM were an offense, when they intruded themselves upon a spirit that was
TOM exalted into the vague august realm of the romantic. No, he would be a
TOM soldier, and return after long years, all war-worn and illustrious. No
TOM -- better still, he would join the Indians, and hunt buffaloes and go on
TOM the warpath in the mountain ranges and the trackless great plains of the
TOM Far West, and away in the future come back a great chief, bristling with
TOM feathers, hideous with paint, and prance into Sunday-school, some drowsy
TOM summer morning, with a blood-curdling war-whoop, and sear the eyeballs
TOM of all his companions with unappeasable envy. But no, there was
TOM something gaudier even than this. He would be a pirate! That was it! NOW
TOM his future lay plain before him, and glowing with unimaginable splendor.
TOM How his name would fill the world, and make people shudder! How
TOM gloriously he would go plowing the dancing seas, in his long, low,
TOM black-hulled racer, the Spirit of the Storm, with his grisly flag flying
TOM at the fore! And at the zenith of his fame, how he would suddenly appear
TOM at the old village and stalk into church, brown and weather-beaten, in
TOM his black velvet doublet and trunks, his great jack-boots, his crimson
TOM sash, his belt bristling with horse-pistols, his crime-rusted cutlass at
TOM his side, his slouch hat with waving plumes, his black flag unfurled,
TOM with the skull and crossbones on it, and hear with swelling ecstasy the
TOM whisperings, ``It's Tom Sawyer the Pirate! -- the Black Avenger of the
TOM Spanish Main!''
TOM 
TOM Yes, it was settled; his career was determined. He would run away from
TOM home and enter upon it. He would start the very next morning. Therefore
TOM he must now begin to get ready. He would collect his resources together.
TOM He went to a rotten log near at hand and began to dig under one end of
TOM it with his Barlow knife. He soon struck wood that sounded hollow. He
TOM put his hand there and uttered this incantation impressively:
TOM 
TOM ``What hasn't come here, come! What's here, stay here!''
TOM 
TOM Then he scraped away the dirt, and exposed a pine shingle. He took it up
TOM and disclosed a shapely little treasure-house whose bottom and sides
TOM were of shingles. In it lay a marble. Tom's astonishment was boundless!
TOM He scratched his head with a perplexed air, and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Well, that beats anything!''
TOM 
TOM Then he tossed the marble away pettishly, and stood cogitating. The
TOM truth was, that a superstition of his had failed, here, which he and all
TOM his comrades had always looked upon as infallible. If you buried a
TOM marble with certain necessary incantations, and left it alone a
TOM fortnight, and then opened the place with the incantation he had just
TOM used, you would find that all the marbles you had ever lost had gathered
TOM themselves together there, meantime, no matter how widely they had been
TOM separated. But now, this thing had actually and unquestionably failed.
TOM Tom's whole structure of faith was shaken to its foundations. He had
TOM many a time heard of this thing succeeding but never of its failing
TOM before. It did not occur to him that he had tried it several times
TOM before, himself, but could never find the hiding-places afterward. He
TOM puzzled over the matter some time, and finally decided that some witch
TOM had interfered and broken the charm. He thought he would satisfy himself
TOM on that point; so he searched around till he found a small sandy spot
TOM with a little funnel-shaped depression in it. He laid himself down and
TOM put his mouth close to this depression and called --
TOM 
TOM ``Doodle-bug, doodle-bug, tell me what I want to know! Doodle-bug,
TOM doodle-bug, tell me what I want to know!''
TOM 
TOM The sand began to work, and presently a small black bug appeared for a
TOM second and then darted under again in a fright.
TOM 
TOM ``He dasn't tell! So it WAS a witch that done it. I just knowed it.''
TOM 
TOM He well knew the futility of trying to contend against witches, so he
TOM gave up discouraged. But it occurred to him that he might as well have
TOM the marble he had just thrown away, and therefore he went and made a
TOM patient search for it. But he could not find it. Now he went back to his
TOM treasure-house and carefully placed himself just as he had been standing
TOM when he tossed the marble away; then he took another marble from his
TOM pocket and tossed it in the same way, saying:
TOM 
TOM ``Brother, go find your brother!''
TOM 
TOM He watched where it stopped, and went there and looked. But it must have
TOM fallen short or gone too far; so he tried twice more. The last
TOM repetition was successful. The two marbles lay within a foot of each
TOM other.
TOM 
TOM Just here the blast of a toy tin trumpet came faintly down the green
TOM aisles of the forest. Tom flung off his jacket and trousers, turned a
TOM suspender into a belt, raked away some brush behind the rotten log,
TOM disclosing a rude bow and arrow, a lath sword and a tin trumpet, and in
TOM a moment had seized these things and bounded away, barelegged, with
TOM fluttering shirt. He presently halted under a great elm, blew an
TOM answering blast, and then began to tiptoe and look warily out, this way
TOM and that. He said cautiously -- to an imaginary company:
TOM 
TOM ``Hold, my merry men! Keep hid till I blow.''
TOM 
TOM Now appeared Joe Harper, as airily clad and elaborately armed as Tom.
TOM Tom called:
TOM 
TOM ``Hold! Who comes here into Sherwood Forest without my pass?''
TOM 
TOM ``Guy of Guisborne wants no man's pass. Who art thou that -- that --''
TOM 
TOM ``Dares to hold such language,'' said Tom, prompting -- for they talked
TOM ``by the book,'' from memory.
TOM 
TOM ``Who art thou that dares to hold such language?''
TOM 
TOM ``I, indeed! I am Robin Hood, as thy caitiff carcase soon shall know.''
TOM 
TOM ``Then art thou indeed that famous outlaw? Right gladly will I dispute
TOM with thee the passes of the merry wood. Have at thee!''
TOM 
TOM They took their lath swords, dumped their other traps on the ground,
TOM struck a fencing attitude, foot to foot, and began a grave, careful
TOM combat, ``two up and two down.'' Presently Tom said:
TOM 
TOM ``Now, if you've got the hang, go it lively!''
TOM 
TOM So they ``went it lively,'' panting and perspiring with the work. By and
TOM by Tom shouted:
TOM 
TOM ``Fall! fall! Why don't you fall?''
TOM 
TOM ``I sha'n't! Why don't you fall yourself? You're getting the worst of
TOM it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, that ain't anything. I can't fall; that ain't the way it is in
TOM the book. The book says, 'Then with one back-handed stroke he slew poor
TOM Guy of Guisborne.' You're to turn around and let me hit you in the
TOM back.''
TOM 
TOM There was no getting around the authorities, so Joe turned, received the
TOM whack and fell.
TOM 
TOM ``Now,'' said Joe, getting up, ``you got to let me kill YOU. That's
TOM fair.''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, I can't do that, it ain't in the book.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, it's blamed mean -- that's all.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, say, Joe, you can be Friar Tuck or Much the miller's son, and
TOM lam me with a quarter-staff; or I'll be the Sheriff of Nottingham and
TOM you be Robin Hood a little while and kill me.''
TOM 
TOM This was satisfactory, and so these adventures were carried out. Then
TOM Tom became Robin Hood again, and was allowed by the treacherous nun to
TOM bleed his strength away through his neglected wound. And at last Joe,
TOM representing a whole tribe of weeping outlaws, dragged him sadly forth,
TOM gave his bow into his feeble hands, and Tom said, ``Where this arrow
TOM falls, there bury poor Robin Hood under the greenwood tree.'' Then he
TOM shot the arrow and fell back and would have died, but he lit on a nettle
TOM and sprang up too gaily for a corpse.
TOM 
TOM The boys dressed themselves, hid their accoutrements, and went off
TOM grieving that there were no outlaws any more, and wondering what modern
TOM civilization could claim to have done to compensate for their loss. They
TOM said they would rather be outlaws a year in Sherwood Forest than
TOM President of the United States forever.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter IX
TOM 
TOM AT half-past nine, that night, Tom and Sid were sent to bed, as usual.
TOM They said their prayers, and Sid was soon asleep. Tom lay awake and
TOM waited, in restless impatience. When it seemed to him that it must be
TOM nearly daylight, he heard the clock strike ten! This was despair. He
TOM would have tossed and fidgeted, as his nerves demanded, but he was
TOM afraid he might wake Sid. So he lay still, and stared up into the dark.
TOM Everything was dismally still. By and by, out of the stillness, little,
TOM scarcely preceptible noises began to emphasize themselves. The ticking
TOM of the clock began to bring itself into notice. Old beams began to crack
TOM mysteriously. The stairs creaked faintly. Evidently spirits were abroad.
TOM A measured, muffled snore issued from Aunt Polly's chamber. And now the
TOM tiresome chirping of a cricket that no human ingenuity could locate,
TOM began. Next the ghastly ticking of a death-watch in the wall at the
TOM bed's head made Tom shudder -- it meant that somebody's days were
TOM numbered. Then the howl of a far-off dog rose on the night air, and was
TOM answered by a fainter howl from a remoter distance. Tom was in an agony.
TOM At last he was satisfied that time had ceased and eternity begun; he
TOM began to doze, in spite of himself; the clock chimed eleven, but he did
TOM not hear it. And then there came, mingling with his half-formed dreams,
TOM a most melancholy caterwauling. The raising of a neighboring window
TOM disturbed him. A cry of ``Scat! you devil!'' and the crash of an empty
TOM bottle against the back of his aunt's woodshed brought him wide awake,
TOM and a single minute later he was dressed and out of the window and
TOM creeping along the roof of the ``ell'' on all fours. He ``meow'd'' with
TOM caution once or twice, as he went; then jumped to the roof of the
TOM woodshed and thence to the ground. Huckleberry Finn was there, with his
TOM dead cat. The boys moved off and disappeared in the gloom. At the end of
TOM half an hour they were wading through the tall grass of the graveyard.
TOM 
TOM It was a graveyard of the old-fashioned Western kind. It was on a hill,
TOM about a mile and a half from the village. It had a crazy board fence
TOM around it, which leaned inward in places, and outward the rest of the
TOM time, but stood upright nowhere. Grass and weeds grew rank over the
TOM whole cemetery. All the old graves were sunken in, there was not a
TOM tombstone on the place; round-topped, worm-eaten boards staggered over
TOM the graves, leaning for support and finding none. ``Sacred to the memory
TOM of'' So-and-So had been painted on them once, but it could no longer
TOM have been read, on the most of them, now, even if there had been light.
TOM 
TOM A faint wind moaned through the trees, and Tom feared it might be the
TOM spirits of the dead, complaining at being disturbed. The boys talked
TOM little, and only under their breath, for the time and the place and the
TOM pervading solemnity and silence oppressed their spirits. They found the
TOM sharp new heap they were seeking, and ensconced themselves within the
TOM protection of three great elms that grew in a bunch within a few feet of
TOM the grave.
TOM 
TOM Then they waited in silence for what seemed a long time. The hooting of
TOM a distant owl was all the sound that troubled the dead stillness. Tom's
TOM reflections grew oppressive. He must force some talk. So he said in a
TOM whisper:
TOM 
TOM ``Hucky, do you believe the dead people like it for us to be here?''
TOM 
TOM Huckleberry whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``I wisht I knowed. It's awful solemn like, AIN'T it?''
TOM 
TOM ``I bet it is.''
TOM 
TOM There was a considerable pause, while the boys canvassed this matter
TOM inwardly. Then Tom whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``Say, Hucky -- do you reckon Hoss Williams hears us talking?''
TOM 
TOM ``O' course he does. Least his sperrit does.''
TOM 
TOM Tom, after a pause:
TOM 
TOM ``I wish I'd said Mister Williams. But I never meant any harm. Everybody
TOM calls him Hoss.''
TOM 
TOM ``A body can't be too partic'lar how they talk 'bout these-yer dead
TOM people, Tom.''
TOM 
TOM This was a damper, and conversation died again.
TOM 
TOM Presently Tom seized his comrade's arm and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Sh!''
TOM 
TOM ``What is it, Tom?'' And the two clung together with beating hearts.
TOM 
TOM ``Sh! There 'tis again! Didn't you hear it?''
TOM 
TOM ``I --''
TOM 
TOM ``There! Now you hear it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Lord, Tom, they're coming! They're coming, sure. What'll we do?''
TOM 
TOM ``I dono. Think they'll see us?''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, Tom, they can see in the dark, same as cats. I wisht I hadn't
TOM come.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, don't be afeard. I don't believe they'll bother us. We ain't doing
TOM any harm. If we keep perfectly still, maybe they won't notice us at
TOM all.''
TOM 
TOM ``I'll try to, Tom, but, Lord, I'm all of a shiver.''
TOM 
TOM ``Listen!''
TOM 
TOM The boys bent their heads together and scarcely breathed. A muffled
TOM sound of voices floated up from the far end of the graveyard.
TOM 
TOM ``Look! See there!'' whispered Tom. ``What is it?''
TOM 
TOM ``It's devil-fire. Oh, Tom, this is awful.''
TOM 
TOM Some vague figures approached through the gloom, swinging an
TOM old-fashioned tin lantern that freckled the ground with innumerable
TOM little spangles of light. Presently Huckleberry whispered with a
TOM shudder:
TOM 
TOM ``It's the devils sure enough. Three of 'em! Lordy, Tom, we're goners!
TOM Can you pray?''
TOM 
TOM ``I'll try, but don't you be afeard. They ain't going to hurt us. 'Now I
TOM lay me down to sleep, I --'''
TOM 
TOM ``Sh!''
TOM 
TOM ``What is it, Huck?''
TOM 
TOM ``They're HUMANS! One of 'em is, anyway. One of 'em's old Muff Potter's
TOM voice.''
TOM 
TOM ``No -- 'tain't so, is it?''
TOM 
TOM ``I bet I know it. Don't you stir nor budge. He ain't sharp enough to
TOM notice us. Drunk, the same as usual, likely -- blamed old rip!''
TOM 
TOM ``All right, I'll keep still. Now they're stuck. Can't find it. Here
TOM they come again. Now they're hot. Cold again. Hot again. Red hot!
TOM They're p'inted right, this time. Say, Huck, I know another o' them
TOM voices; it's Injun Joe.''
TOM 
TOM ``That's so -- that murderin' half-breed! I'd druther they was devils a
TOM dern sight. What kin they be up to?''
TOM 
TOM The whisper died wholly out, now, for the three men had reached the
TOM grave and stood within a few feet of the boys' hiding-place.
TOM 
TOM ``Here it is,'' said the third voice; and the owner of it held the
TOM lantern up and revealed the face of young Doctor Robinson.
TOM 
TOM Potter and Injun Joe were carrying a handbarrow with a rope and a couple
TOM of shovels on it. They cast down their load and began to open the grave.
TOM The doctor put the lantern at the head of the grave and came and sat
TOM down with his back against one of the elm trees. He was so close the
TOM boys could have touched him.
TOM 
TOM ``Hurry, men!'' he said, in a low voice; ``the moon might come out at
TOM any moment.''
TOM 
TOM They growled a response and went on digging. For some time there was no
TOM noise but the grating sound of the spades discharging their freight of
TOM mould and gravel. It was very monotonous. Finally a spade struck upon
TOM the coffin with a dull woody accent, and within another minute or two
TOM the men had hoisted it out on the ground. They pried off the lid with
TOM their shovels, got out the body and dumped it rudely on the ground. The
TOM moon drifted from behind the clouds and exposed the pallid face. The
TOM barrow was got ready and the corpse placed on it, covered with a
TOM blanket, and bound to its place with the rope. Potter took out a large
TOM spring-knife and cut off the dangling end of the rope and then said:
TOM 
TOM ``Now the cussed thing's ready, Sawbones, and you'll just out with
TOM another five, or here she stays.''
TOM 
TOM ``That's the talk!'' said Injun Joe.
TOM 
TOM ``Look here, what does this mean?'' said the doctor. ``You required your
TOM pay in advance, and I've paid you.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, and you done more than that,'' said Injun Joe, approaching the
TOM doctor, who was now standing. ``Five years ago you drove me away from
TOM your father's kitchen one night, when I come to ask for something to
TOM eat, and you said I warn't there for any good; and when I swore I'd get
TOM even with you if it took a hundred years, your father had me jailed for
TOM a vagrant. Did you think I'd forget? The Injun blood ain't in me for
TOM nothing. And now I've GOT you, and you got to SETTLE, you know!''
TOM 
TOM He was threatening the doctor, with his fist in his face, by this time.
TOM The doctor struck out suddenly and stretched the ruffian on the ground.
TOM Potter dropped his knife, and exclaimed:
TOM 
TOM ``Here, now, don't you hit my pard!'' and the next moment he had
TOM grappled with the doctor and the two were struggling with might and
TOM main, trampling the grass and tearing the ground with their heels. Injun
TOM Joe sprang to his feet, his eyes flaming with passion, snatched up
TOM Potter's knife, and went creeping, catlike and stooping, round and round
TOM about the combatants, seeking an opportunity. All at once the doctor
TOM flung himself free, seized the heavy headboard of Williams' grave and
TOM felled Potter to the earth with it -- and in the same instant the
TOM half-breed saw his chance and drove the knife to the hilt in the young
TOM man's breast. He reeled and fell partly upon Potter, flooding him with
TOM his blood, and in the same moment the clouds blotted out the dreadful
TOM spectacle and the two frightened boys went speeding away in the dark.
TOM 
TOM Presently, when the moon emerged again, Injun Joe was standing over the
TOM two forms, contemplating them. The doctor murmured inarticulately, gave
TOM a long gasp or two and was still. The half-breed muttered:
TOM 
TOM ``THAT score is settled -- damn you.''
TOM 
TOM Then he robbed the body. After which he put the fatal knife in Potter's
TOM open right hand, and sat down on the dismantled coffin. Three -- four --
TOM five minutes passed, and then Potter began to stir and moan. His hand
TOM closed upon the knife; he raised it, glanced at it, and let it fall,
TOM with a shudder. Then he sat up, pushing the body from him, and gazed at
TOM it, and then around him, confusedly. His eyes met Joe's.
TOM 
TOM ``Lord, how is this, Joe?'' he said.
TOM 
TOM ``It's a dirty business,'' said Joe, without moving.
TOM 
TOM ``What did you do it for?''
TOM 
TOM ``I! I never done it!''
TOM 
TOM ``Look here! That kind of talk won't wash.''
TOM 
TOM Potter trembled and grew white.
TOM 
TOM ``I thought I'd got sober. I'd no business to drink to-night. But it's
TOM in my head yet -- worse'n when we started here. I'm all in a muddle;
TOM can't recollect anything of it, hardly. Tell me, Joe -- HONEST, now, old
TOM feller -- did I do it? Joe, I never meant to -- 'pon my soul and honor,
TOM I never meant to, Joe. Tell me how it was, Joe. Oh, it's awful -- and
TOM him so young and promising.''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, you two was scuffling, and he fetched you one with the headboard
TOM and you fell flat; and then up you come, all reeling and staggering
TOM like, and snatched the knife and jammed it into him, just as he fetched
TOM you another awful clip -- and here you've laid, as dead as a wedge til
TOM now.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, I didn't know what I was a-doing. I wish I may die this minute if
TOM I did. It was all on account of the whiskey and the excitement, I
TOM reckon. I never used a weepon in my life before, Joe. I've fought, but
TOM never with weepons. They'll all say that. Joe, don't tell! Say you won't
TOM tell, Joe -- that's a good feller. I always liked you, Joe, and stood up
TOM for you, too. Don't you remember? You WON'T tell, WILL you, Joe?'' And
TOM the poor creature dropped on his knees before the stolid murderer, and
TOM clasped his appealing hands.
TOM 
TOM ``No, you've always been fair and square with me, Muff Potter, and I
TOM won't go back on you. There, now, that's as fair as a man can say.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, Joe, you're an angel. I'll bless you for this the longest day I
TOM live.'' And Potter began to cry.
TOM 
TOM ``Come, now, that's enough of that. This ain't any time for blubbering.
TOM You be off yonder way and I'll go this. Move, now, and don't leave any
TOM tracks behind you.''
TOM 
TOM Potter started on a trot that quickly increased to a run. The half-breed
TOM stood looking after him. He muttered:
TOM 
TOM ``If he's as much stunned with the lick and fuddled with the rum as he
TOM had the look of being, he won't think of the knife till he's gone so far
TOM he'll be afraid to come back after it to such a place by himself --
TOM chicken-heart!''
TOM 
TOM Two or three minutes later the murdered man, the blanketed corpse, the
TOM lidless coffin, and the open grave were under no inspection but the
TOM moon's. The stillness was complete again, too.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter X
TOM 
TOM THE two boys flew on and on, toward the village, speechless with horror.
TOM They glanced backward over their shoulders from time to time,
TOM apprehensively, as if they feared they might be followed. Every stump
TOM that started up in their path seemed a man and an enemy, and made them
TOM catch their breath; and as they sped by some outlying cottages that lay
TOM near the village, the barking of the aroused watch-dogs seemed to give
TOM wings to their feet.
TOM 
TOM ``If we can only get to the old tannery before we break down!''
TOM whispered Tom, in short catches between breaths. ``I can't stand it much
TOM longer.''
TOM 
TOM Huckleberry's hard pantings were his only reply, and the boys fixed
TOM their eyes on the goal of their hopes and bent to their work to win it.
TOM They gained steadily on it, and at last, breast to breast, they burst
TOM through the open door and fell grateful and exhausted in the sheltering
TOM shadows beyond. By and by their pulses slowed down, and Tom whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``Huckleberry, what do you reckon'll come of this?''
TOM 
TOM ``If Doctor Robinson dies, I reckon hanging'll come of it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Do you though?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, I KNOW it, Tom.''
TOM 
TOM Tom thought a while, then he said:
TOM 
TOM ``Who'll tell? We?''
TOM 
TOM ``What are you talking about? S'pose something happened and Injun Joe
TOM DIDN'T hang? Why, he'd kill us some time or other, just as dead sure as
TOM we're a laying here.''
TOM 
TOM ``That's just what I was thinking to myself, Huck.''
TOM 
TOM ``If anybody tells, let Muff Potter do it, if he's fool enough. He's
TOM generally drunk enough.''
TOM 
TOM Tom said nothing -- went on thinking. Presently he whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``Huck, Muff Potter don't know it. How can he tell?''
TOM 
TOM ``What's the reason he don't know it?''
TOM 
TOM ``Because he'd just got that whack when Injun Joe done it. D'you reckon
TOM he could see anything? D'you reckon he knowed anything?''
TOM 
TOM ``By hokey, that's so, Tom!''
TOM 
TOM ``And besides, look-a-here -- maybe that whack done for HIM!''
TOM 
TOM ``No, 'taint likely, Tom. He had liquor in him; I could see that; and
TOM besides, he always has. Well, when pap's full, you might take and belt
TOM him over the head with a church and you couldn't phase him. He says so,
TOM his own self. So it's the same with Muff Potter, of course. But if a man
TOM was dead sober, I reckon maybe that whack might fetch him; I dono.''
TOM 
TOM After another reflective silence, Tom said:
TOM 
TOM ``Hucky, you sure you can keep mum?''
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, we GOT to keep mum. You know that. That Injun devil wouldn't make
TOM any more of drownding us than a couple of cats, if we was to squeak
TOM 'bout this and they didn't hang him. Now, look-a-here, Tom, less take
TOM and swear to one another -- that's what we got to do -- swear to keep
TOM mum.''
TOM 
TOM ``I'm agreed. It's the best thing. Would you just hold hands and swear
TOM that we --''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh no, that wouldn't do for this. That's good enough for little
TOM rubbishy common things -- specially with gals, cuz THEY go back on you
TOM anyway, and blab if they get in a huff -- but there orter be writing
TOM 'bout a big thing like this. And blood.''
TOM 
TOM Tom's whole being applauded this idea. It was deep, and dark, and awful;
TOM the hour, the circumstances, the surroundings, were in keeping with it.
TOM He picked up a clean pine shingle that lay in the moon-light, took a
TOM little fragment of ``red keel'' out of his pocket, got the moon on his
TOM work, and painfully scrawled these lines, emphasizing each slow
TOM down-stroke by clamping his tongue between his teeth, and letting up the
TOM pressure on the up-strokes. [See next page.]
TOM 
TOM ``Huck Finn and Tom Sawyer swears they will keep mum about This and They
TOM wish They may Drop down dead in Their Tracks if They ever Tell and Rot.''
TOM 
TOM Huckleberry was filled with admiration of Tom's facility in writing, and
TOM the sublimity of his language. He at once took a pin from his lapel and
TOM was going to prick his flesh, but Tom said:
TOM 
TOM ``Hold on! Don't do that. A pin's brass. It might have verdigrease on
TOM it.''
TOM 
TOM ``What's verdigrease?''
TOM 
TOM ``It's p'ison. That's what it is. You just swaller some of it once --
TOM you'll see.''
TOM 
TOM So Tom unwound the thread from one of his needles, and each boy pricked
TOM the ball of his thumb and squeezed out a drop of blood. In time, after
TOM many squeezes, Tom managed to sign his initials, using the ball of his
TOM little finger for a pen. Then he showed Huckleberry how to make an H and
TOM an F, and the oath was complete. They buried the shingle close to the
TOM wall, with some dismal ceremonies and incantations, and the fetters that
TOM bound their tongues were considered to be locked and the key thrown
TOM away.
TOM 
TOM A figure crept stealthily through a break in the other end of the ruined
TOM building, now, but they did not notice it.
TOM 
TOM ``Tom,'' whispered Huckleberry, ``does this keep us from EVER telling --
TOM ALWAYS?''
TOM 
TOM ``Of course it does. It don't make any difference WHAT happens, we got
TOM to keep mum. We'd drop down dead -- don't YOU know that?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, I reckon that's so.''
TOM 
TOM They continued to whisper for some little time. Presently a dog set up a
TOM long, lugubrious howl just outside -- within ten feet of them. The boys
TOM clasped each other suddenly, in an agony of fright.
TOM 
TOM ``Which of us does he mean?'' gasped Huckleberry.
TOM 
TOM ``I dono -- peep through the crack. Quick!''
TOM 
TOM ``No, YOU, Tom!''
TOM 
TOM ``I can't -- I can't DO it, Huck!''
TOM 
TOM ``Please, Tom. There 'tis again!''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, lordy, I'm thankful!'' whispered Tom. ``I know his voice. It's
TOM Bull Harbison.'' *
TOM 
TOM [* If Mr. Harbison owned a slave named Bull, Tom would have spoken of
TOM him as ``Harbison's Bull,'' but a son or a dog of that name was ``Bull
TOM Harbison.'']
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, that's good -- I tell you, Tom, I was most scared to death; I'd a
TOM bet anything it was a STRAY dog.''
TOM 
TOM The dog howled again. The boys' hearts sank once more.
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, my! that ain't no Bull Harbison!'' whispered Huckleberry. ``DO,
TOM Tom!''
TOM 
TOM Tom, quaking with fear, yielded, and put his eye to the crack. His
TOM whisper was hardly audible when he said:
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, Huck, IT S A STRAY DOG!''
TOM 
TOM ``Quick, Tom, quick! Who does he mean?''
TOM 
TOM ``Huck, he must mean us both -- we're right together.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, Tom, I reckon we're goners. I reckon there ain't no mistake 'bout
TOM where I'LL go to. I been so wicked.''
TOM 
TOM ``Dad fetch it! This comes of playing hookey and doing everything a
TOM feller's told NOT to do. I might a been good, like Sid, if I'd a tried
TOM -- but no, I wouldn't, of course. But if ever I get off this time, I lay
TOM I'll just WALLER in Sunday-schools!'' And Tom began to snuffle a little.
TOM 
TOM ``YOU bad!'' and Huckleberry began to snuffle too. ``Consound it, Tom
TOM Sawyer, you're just old pie, 'longside o' what I am. Oh, LORDY, lordy,
TOM lordy, I wisht I only had half your chance.''
TOM 
TOM Tom choked off and whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``Look, Hucky, look! He's got his BACK to us!''
TOM 
TOM Hucky looked, with joy in his heart.
TOM 
TOM ``Well, he has, by jingoes! Did he before?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, he did. But I, like a fool, never thought. Oh, this is bully, you
TOM know. NOW who can he mean?''
TOM 
TOM The howling stopped. Tom pricked up his ears.
TOM 
TOM ``Sh! What's that?'' he whispered.
TOM 
TOM ``Sounds like -- like hogs grunting. No -- it's somebody snoring, Tom.''
TOM 
TOM ``That IS it! Where 'bouts is it, Huck?''
TOM 
TOM ``I bleeve it's down at 'tother end. Sounds so, anyway. Pap used to
TOM sleep there, sometimes, 'long with the hogs, but laws bless you, he just
TOM lifts things when HE snores. Besides, I reckon he ain't ever coming back
TOM to this town any more.''
TOM 
TOM The spirit of adventure rose in the boys' souls once more.
TOM 
TOM ``Hucky, do you das't to go if I lead?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't like to, much. Tom, s'pose it's Injun Joe!''
TOM 
TOM Tom quailed. But presently the temptation rose up strong again and the
TOM boys agreed to try, with the understanding that they would take to their
TOM heels if the snoring stopped. So they went tiptoeing stealthily down,
TOM the one behind the other. When they had got to within five steps of the
TOM snorer, Tom stepped on a stick, and it broke with a sharp snap. The man
TOM moaned, writhed a little, and his face came into the moonlight. It was
TOM Muff Potter. The boys' hearts had stood still, and their hopes too, when
TOM the man moved, but their fears passed away now. They tiptoed out,
TOM through the broken weather-boarding, and stopped at a little distance to
TOM exchange a parting word. That long, lugubrious howl rose on the night
TOM air again! They turned and saw the strange dog standing within a few
TOM feet of where Potter was lying, and FACING Potter, with his nose
TOM pointing heavenward.
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, geeminy, it's HIM!'' exclaimed both boys, in a breath.
TOM 
TOM ``Say, Tom -- they say a stray dog come howling around Johnny Miller's
TOM house, 'bout midnight, as much as two weeks ago; and a whippoorwill come
TOM in and lit on the banisters and sung, the very same evening; and there
TOM ain't anybody dead there yet.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I know that. And suppose there ain't. Didn't Gracie Miller fall
TOM in the kitchen fire and burn herself terrible the very next Saturday?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, but she ain't DEAD. And what's more, she's getting better, too.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right, you wait and see. She's a goner, just as dead sure as Muff
TOM Potter's a goner. That's what the niggers say, and they know all about
TOM these kind of things, Huck.''
TOM 
TOM Then they separated, cogitating. When Tom crept in at his bedroom window
TOM the night was almost spent. He undressed with excessive caution, and
TOM fell asleep congratulating himself that nobody knew of his escapade. He
TOM was not aware that the gently-snoring Sid was awake, and had been so for
TOM an hour.
TOM 
TOM When Tom awoke, Sid was dressed and gone. There was a late look in the
TOM light, a late sense in the atmosphere. He was startled. Why had he not
TOM been called -- persecuted till he was up, as usual? The thought filled
TOM him with bodings. Within five minutes he was dressed and down-stairs,
TOM feeling sore and drowsy. The family were still at table, but they had
TOM finished breakfast. There was no voice of rebuke; but there were averted
TOM eyes; there was a silence and an air of solemnity that struck a chill to
TOM the culprit's heart. He sat down and tried to seem gay, but it was
TOM up-hill work; it roused no smile, no response, and he lapsed into
TOM silence and let his heart sink down to the depths.
TOM 
TOM After breakfast his aunt took him aside, and Tom almost brightened in
TOM the hope that he was going to be flogged; but it was not so. His aunt
TOM wept over him and asked him how he could go and break her old heart so;
TOM and finally told him to go on, and ruin himself and bring her gray hairs
TOM with sorrow to the grave, for it was no use for her to try any more.
TOM This was worse than a thousand whippings, and Tom's heart was sorer now
TOM than his body. He cried, he pleaded for forgiveness, promised to reform
TOM over and over again, and then received his dismissal, feeling that he
TOM had won but an imperfect forgiveness and established but a feeble
TOM confidence.
TOM 
TOM He left the presence too miserable to even feel revengeful toward Sid;
TOM and so the latter's prompt retreat through the back gate was
TOM unnecessary. He moped to school gloomy and sad, and took his flogging,
TOM along with Joe Harper, for playing hookey the day before, with the air
TOM of one whose heart was busy with heavier woes and wholly dead to
TOM trifles. Then he betook himself to his seat, rested his elbows on his
TOM desk and his jaws in his hands, and stared at the wall with the stony
TOM stare of suffering that has reached the limit and can no further go. His
TOM elbow was pressing against some hard substance. After a long time he
TOM slowly and sadly changed his position, and took up this object with a
TOM sigh. It was in a paper. He unrolled it. A long, lingering, colossal
TOM sigh followed, and his heart broke. It was his brass andiron knob!
TOM 
TOM This final feather broke the camel's back.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XI
TOM 
TOM CLOSE upon the hour of noon the whole village was suddenly electrified
TOM with the ghastly news. No need of the as yet undreamed-of telegraph; the
TOM tale flew from man to man, from group to group, from house to house,
TOM with little less than telegraphic speed. Of course the schoolmaster gave
TOM holiday for that afternoon; the town would have thought strangely of him
TOM if he had not.
TOM 
TOM A gory knife had been found close to the murdered man, and it had been
TOM recognized by somebody as belonging to Muff Potter -- so the story ran.
TOM And it was said that a belated citizen had come upon Potter washing
TOM himself in the ``branch'' about one or two o'clock in the morning, and
TOM that Potter had at once sneaked off -- suspicious circumstances,
TOM especially the washing which was not a habit with Potter. It was also
TOM said that the town had been ransacked for this ``murderer'' (the public
TOM are not slow in the matter of sifting evidence and arriving at a
TOM verdict), but that he could not be found. Horsemen had departed down all
TOM the roads in every direction, and the Sheriff ``was confident'' that he
TOM would be captured before night.
TOM 
TOM All the town was drifting toward the graveyard. Tom's heartbreak
TOM vanished and he joined the procession, not because he would not a
TOM thousand times rather go anywhere else, but because an awful,
TOM unaccountable fascination drew him on. Arrived at the dreadful place, he
TOM wormed his small body through the crowd and saw the dismal spectacle. It
TOM seemed to him an age since he was there before. Somebody pinched his
TOM arm. He turned, and his eyes met Huckleberry's. Then both looked
TOM elsewhere at once, and wondered if anybody had noticed anything in their
TOM mutual glance. But everybody was talking, and intent upon the grisly
TOM spectacle before them.
TOM 
TOM ``Poor fellow!'' ``Poor young fellow!'' ``This ought to be a lesson to
TOM grave robbers!'' ``Muff Potter'll hang for this if they catch him!''
TOM This was the drift of remark; and the minister said, ``It was a
TOM judgment; His hand is here.''
TOM 
TOM Now Tom shivered from head to heel; for his eye fell upon the stolid
TOM face of Injun Joe. At this moment the crowd began to sway and struggle,
TOM and voices shouted, ``It's him! it's him! he's coming himself!''
TOM 
TOM ``Who? Who?'' from twenty voices.
TOM 
TOM ``Muff Potter!''
TOM 
TOM ``Hallo, he's stopped! -- Look out, he's turning! Don't let him get
TOM away!''
TOM 
TOM People in the branches of the trees over Tom's head said he wasn't
TOM trying to get away -- he only looked doubtful and perplexed.
TOM 
TOM ``Infernal impudence!'' said a bystander; ``wanted to come and take a
TOM quiet look at his work, I reckon -- didn't expect any company.''
TOM 
TOM The crowd fell apart, now, and the Sheriff came through, ostentatiously
TOM leading Potter by the arm. The poor fellow's face was haggard, and his
TOM eyes showed the fear that was upon him. When he stood before the
TOM murdered man, he shook as with a palsy, and he put his face in his hands
TOM and burst into tears.
TOM 
TOM ``I didn't do it, friends,'' he sobbed; ``'pon my word and honor I never
TOM done it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Who's accused you?'' shouted a voice.
TOM 
TOM This shot seemed to carry home. Potter lifted his face and looked around
TOM him with a pathetic hopelessness in his eyes. He saw Injun Joe, and
TOM exclaimed:
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, Injun Joe, you promised me you'd never --''
TOM 
TOM ``Is that your knife?'' and it was thrust before him by the Sheriff.
TOM 
TOM Potter would have fallen if they had not caught him and eased him to the
TOM ground. Then he said:
TOM 
TOM ``Something told me 't if I didn't come back and get --'' He shuddered;
TOM then waved his nerveless hand with a vanquished gesture and said, ``Tell
TOM 'em, Joe, tell 'em -- it ain't any use any more.''
TOM 
TOM Then Huckleberry and Tom stood dumb and staring, and heard the
TOM stony-hearted liar reel off his serene statement, they expecting every
TOM moment that the clear sky would deliver God's lightnings upon his head,
TOM and wondering to see how long the stroke was delayed. And when he had
TOM finished and still stood alive and whole, their wavering impulse to
TOM break their oath and save the poor betrayed prisoner's life faded and
TOM vanished away, for plainly this miscreant had sold himself to Satan and
TOM it would be fatal to meddle with the property of such a power as that.
TOM 
TOM ``Why didn't you leave? What did you want to come here for?'' somebody
TOM said.
TOM 
TOM ``I couldn't help it -- I couldn't help it,'' Potter moaned. ``I wanted
TOM to run away, but I couldn't seem to come anywhere but here.'' And he
TOM fell to sobbing again.
TOM 
TOM Injun Joe repeated his statement, just as calmly, a few minutes
TOM afterward on the inquest, under oath; and the boys, seeing that the
TOM lightnings were still withheld, were confirmed in their belief that Joe
TOM had sold himself to the devil. He was now become, to them, the most
TOM balefully interesting object they had ever looked upon, and they could
TOM not take their fascinated eyes from his face.
TOM 
TOM They inwardly resolved to watch him nights, when opportunity should
TOM offer, in the hope of getting a glimpse of his dread master.
TOM 
TOM Injun Joe helped to raise the body of the murdered man and put it in a
TOM wagon for removal; and it was whispered through the shuddering crowd
TOM that the wound bled a little! The boys thought that this happy
TOM circumstance would turn suspicion in the right direction; but they were
TOM disappointed, for more than one villager remarked:
TOM 
TOM ``It was within three feet of Muff Potter when it done it.''
TOM 
TOM Tom's fearful secret and gnawing conscience disturbed his sleep for as
TOM much as a week after this; and at breakfast one morning Sid said:
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, you pitch around and talk in your sleep so much that you keep me
TOM awake half the time.''
TOM 
TOM Tom blanched and dropped his eyes.
TOM 
TOM ``It's a bad sign,'' said Aunt Polly, gravely. ``What you got on your
TOM mind, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``Nothing. Nothing 't I know of.'' But the boy's hand shook so that he
TOM spilled his coffee.
TOM 
TOM ``And you do talk such stuff,'' Sid said. ``Last night you said, 'It's
TOM blood, it's blood, that's what it is!' You said that over and over. And
TOM you said, 'Don't torment me so -- I'll tell!' Tell WHAT? What is it
TOM you'll tell?''
TOM 
TOM Everything was swimming before Tom. There is no telling what might have
TOM happened, now, but luckily the concern passed out of Aunt Polly's face
TOM and she came to Tom's relief without knowing it. She said:
TOM 
TOM ``Sho! It's that dreadful murder. I dream about it most every night
TOM myself. Sometimes I dream it's me that done it.''
TOM 
TOM Mary said she had been affected much the same way. Sid seemed satisfied.
TOM Tom got out of the presence as quick as he plausibly could, and after
TOM that he complained of toothache for a week, and tied up his jaws every
TOM night. He never knew that Sid lay nightly watching, and frequently
TOM slipped the bandage free and then leaned on his elbow listening a good
TOM while at a time, and afterward slipped the bandage back to its place
TOM again. Tom's distress of mind wore off gradually and the toothache grew
TOM irksome and was discarded. If Sid really managed to make anything out of
TOM Tom's disjointed mutterings, he kept it to himself.
TOM 
TOM It seemed to Tom that his schoolmates never would get done holding
TOM inquests on dead cats, and thus keeping his trouble present to his mind.
TOM Sid noticed that Tom never was coroner at one of these inquiries, though
TOM it had been his habit to take the lead in all new enterprises; he
TOM noticed, too, that Tom never acted as a witness -- and that was strange;
TOM and Sid did not overlook the fact that Tom even showed a marked aversion
TOM to these inquests, and always avoided them when he could. Sid marvelled,
TOM but said nothing. However, even inquests went out of vogue at last, and
TOM ceased to torture Tom's conscience.
TOM 
TOM Every day or two, during this time of sorrow, Tom watched his
TOM opportunity and went to the little grated jail-window and smuggled such
TOM small comforts through to the ``murderer'' as he could get hold of. The
TOM jail was a trifling little brick den that stood in a marsh at the edge
TOM of the village, and no guards were afforded for it; indeed, it was
TOM seldom occupied. These offerings greatly helped to ease Tom's
TOM conscience.
TOM 
TOM The villagers had a strong desire to tar-and-feather Injun Joe and ride
TOM him on a rail, for body-snatching, but so formidable was his character
TOM that nobody could be found who was willing to take the lead in the
TOM matter, so it was dropped. He had been careful to begin both of his
TOM inquest-statements with the fight, without confessing the grave-robbery
TOM that preceded it; therefore it was deemed wisest not to try the case in
TOM the courts at present.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XII
TOM 
TOM ONE of the reasons why Tom's mind had drifted away from its secret
TOM troubles was, that it had found a new and weighty matter to interest
TOM itself about. Becky Thatcher had stopped coming to school. Tom had
TOM struggled with his pride a few days, and tried to ``whistle her down the
TOM wind,'' but failed. He began to find himself hanging around her father's
TOM house, nights, and feeling very miserable. She was ill. What if she
TOM should die! There was distraction in the thought. He no longer took an
TOM interest in war, nor even in piracy. The charm of life was gone; there
TOM was nothing but dreariness left. He put his hoop away, and his bat;
TOM there was no joy in them any more. His aunt was concerned. She began to
TOM try all manner of remedies on him. She was one of those people who are
TOM infatuated with patent medicines and all new-fangled methods of
TOM producing health or mending it. She was an inveterate experimenter in
TOM these things. When something fresh in this line came out she was in a
TOM fever, right away, to try it; not on herself, for she was never ailing,
TOM but on anybody else that came handy. She was a subscriber for all the
TOM ``Health'' periodicals and phrenological frauds; and the solemn
TOM ignorance they were inflated with was breath to her nostrils. All the
TOM ``rot'' they contained about ventilation, and how to go to bed, and how
TOM to get up, and what to eat, and what to drink, and how much exercise to
TOM take, and what frame of mind to keep one's self in, and what sort of
TOM clothing to wear, was all gospel to her, and she never observed that her
TOM health-journals of the current month customarily upset everything they
TOM had recommended the month before. She was as simple-hearted and honest
TOM as the day was long, and so she was an easy victim. She gathered
TOM together her quack periodicals and her quack medicines, and thus armed
TOM with death, went about on her pale horse, metaphorically speaking, with
TOM ``hell following after.'' But she never suspected that she was not an
TOM angel of healing and the balm of Gilead in disguise, to the suffering
TOM neighbors.
TOM 
TOM The water treatment was new, now, and Tom's low condition was a windfall
TOM to her. She had him out at daylight every morning, stood him up in the
TOM wood-shed and drowned him with a deluge of cold water; then she scrubbed
TOM him down with a towel like a file, and so brought him to; then she
TOM rolled him up in a wet sheet and put him away under blankets till she
TOM sweated his soul clean and ``the yellow stains of it came through his
TOM pores'' -- as Tom said.
TOM 
TOM Yet notwithstanding all this, the boy grew more and more melancholy and
TOM pale and dejected. She added hot baths, sitz baths, shower baths, and
TOM plunges. The boy remained as dismal as a hearse. She began to assist the
TOM water with a slim oatmeal diet and blister-plasters. She calculated his
TOM capacity as she would a jug's, and filled him up every day with quack
TOM cure-alls.
TOM 
TOM Tom had become indifferent to persecution by this time. This phase
TOM filled the old lady's heart with consternation. This indifference must
TOM be broken up at any cost. Now she heard of Pain-killer for the first
TOM time. She ordered a lot at once. She tasted it and was filled with
TOM gratitude. It was simply fire in a liquid form. She dropped the water
TOM treatment and everything else, and pinned her faith to Pain-killer. She
TOM gave Tom a teaspoonful and watched with the deepest anxiety for the
TOM result. Her troubles were instantly at rest, her soul at peace again;
TOM for the ``indifference'' was broken up. The boy could not have shown a
TOM wilder, heartier interest, if she had built a fire under him.
TOM 
TOM Tom felt that it was time to wake up; this sort of life might be
TOM romantic enough, in his blighted condition, but it was getting to have
TOM too little sentiment and too much distracting variety about it. So he
TOM thought over various plans for relief, and finally hit pon that of
TOM professing to be fond of Pain-killer. He asked for it so often that he
TOM became a nuisance, and his aunt ended by telling him to help himself and
TOM quit bothering her. If it had been Sid, she would have had no misgivings
TOM to alloy her delight; but since it was Tom, she watched the bottle
TOM clandestinely. She found that the medicine did really diminish, but it
TOM did not occur to her that the boy was mending the health of a crack in
TOM the sitting-room floor with it.
TOM 
TOM One day Tom was in the act of dosing the crack when his aunt's yellow
TOM cat came along, purring, eying the teaspoon avariciously, and begging
TOM for a taste. Tom said:
TOM 
TOM ``Don't ask for it unless you want it, Peter.''
TOM 
TOM But Peter signified that he did want it.
TOM 
TOM ``You better make sure.''
TOM 
TOM Peter was sure.
TOM 
TOM ``Now you've asked for it, and I'll give it to you, because there ain't
TOM anything mean about me; but if you find you don't like it, you mustn't
TOM blame anybody but your own self.''
TOM 
TOM Peter was agreeable. So Tom pried his mouth open and poured down the
TOM Pain-killer. Peter sprang a couple of yards in the air, and then
TOM delivered a war-whoop and set off round and round the room, banging
TOM against furniture, upsetting flower-pots, and making general havoc. Next
TOM he rose on his hind feet and pranced around, in a frenzy of enjoyment,
TOM with his head over his shoulder and his voice proclaiming his
TOM unappeasable happiness. Then he went tearing around the house again
TOM spreading chaos and destruction in his path. Aunt Polly entered in time
TOM to see him throw a few double summersets, deliver a final mighty hurrah,
TOM and sail through the open window, carrying the rest of the flower-pots
TOM with him. The old lady stood petrified with astonishment, peering over
TOM her glasses; Tom lay on the floor expiring with laughter.
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, what on earth ails that cat?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't know, aunt,'' gasped the boy.
TOM 
TOM ``Why, I never see anything like it. What did make him act so?''
TOM 
TOM ``Deed I don't know, Aunt Polly; cats always act so when they're having
TOM a good time.''
TOM 
TOM ``They do, do they?'' There was something in the tone that made Tom
TOM apprehensive.
TOM 
TOM ``Yes'm. That is, I believe they do.''
TOM 
TOM ``You DO?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes'm.''
TOM 
TOM The old lady was bending down, Tom watching, with interest emphasized by
TOM anxiety. Too late he divined her ``drift.'' The handle of the telltale
TOM teaspoon was visible under the bed-valance. Aunt Polly took it, held it
TOM up. Tom winced, and dropped his eyes. Aunt Polly raised him by the usual
TOM handle -- his ear -- and cracked his head soundly with her thimble.
TOM 
TOM ``Now, sir, what did you want to treat that poor dumb beast so, for?''
TOM 
TOM ``I done it out of pity for him -- because he hadn't any aunt.''
TOM 
TOM ``Hadn't any aunt! -- you numskull. What has that got to do with it?''
TOM 
TOM ``Heaps. Because if he'd had one she'd a burnt him out herself! She'd a
TOM roasted his bowels out of him 'thout any more feeling than if he was a
TOM human!''
TOM 
TOM Aunt Polly felt a sudden pang of remorse. This was putting the thing in
TOM a new light; what was cruelty to a cat MIGHT be cruelty to a boy, too.
TOM She began to soften; she felt sorry. Her eyes watered a little, and she
TOM put her hand on Tom's head and said gently:
TOM 
TOM ``I was meaning for the best, Tom. And, Tom, it DID do you good.''
TOM 
TOM Tom looked up in her face with just a perceptible twinkle peeping
TOM through his gravity.
TOM 
TOM ``I know you was meaning for the best, aunty, and so was I with Peter.
TOM It done HIM good, too. I never see him get around so since --''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, go 'long with you, Tom, before you aggravate me again. And you try
TOM and see if you can't be a good boy, for once, and you needn't take any
TOM more medicine.''
TOM 
TOM Tom reached school ahead of time. It was noticed that this strange thing
TOM had been occurring every day latterly. And now, as usual of late, he
TOM hung about the gate of the schoolyard instead of playing with his
TOM comrades. He was sick, he said, and he looked it. He tried to seem to be
TOM looking everywhere but whither he really was looking -- down the road.
TOM Presently Jeff Thatcher hove in sight, and Tom's face lighted; he gazed
TOM a moment, and then turned sorrowfully away. When Jeff arrived, Tom
TOM accosted him; and ``led up'' warily to opportunities for remark about
TOM Becky, but the giddy lad never could see the bait. Tom watched and
TOM watched, hoping whenever a frisking frock came in sight, and hating the
TOM owner of it as soon as he saw she was not the right one. At last frocks
TOM ceased to appear, and he dropped hopelessly into the dumps; he entered
TOM the empty schoolhouse and sat down to suffer. Then one more frock passed
TOM in at the gate, and Tom's heart gave a great bound. The next instant he
TOM was out, and ``going on'' like an Indian; yelling, laughing, chasing
TOM boys, jumping over the fence at risk of life and limb, throwing
TOM handsprings, standing on his head -- doing all the heroic things he
TOM could conceive of, and keeping a furtive eye out, all the while, to see
TOM if Becky Thatcher was noticing. But she seemed to be unconscious of it
