TOM Alfred dropped alongside and was going to try to comfort her, but she
TOM said:
TOM 
TOM ``Go away and leave me alone, can't you! I hate you!''
TOM 
TOM So the boy halted, wondering what he could have done -- for she had said
TOM she would look at pictures all through the nooning -- and she walked on,
TOM crying. Then Alfred went musing into the deserted school-house. He was
TOM humiliated and angry. He easily guessed his way to the truth -- the girl
TOM had simply made a convenience of him to vent her spite upon Tom Sawyer.
TOM He was far from hating Tom the less when this thought occurred to him.
TOM He wished there was some way to get that boy into trouble without much
TOM risk to himself. Tom's spelling-book fell under his eye. Here was his
TOM opportunity. He gratefully opened to the lesson for the afternoon and
TOM poured ink upon the page.
TOM 
TOM Becky, glancing in at a window behind him at the moment, saw the act,
TOM and moved on, without discovering herself. She started homeward, now,
TOM intending to find Tom and tell him; Tom would be thankful and their
TOM troubles would be healed. Before she was half way home, however, she had
TOM changed her mind. The thought of Tom's treatment of her when she was
TOM talking about her picnic came scorching back and filled her with shame.
TOM She resolved to let him get whipped on the damaged spelling-book's
TOM account, and to hate him forever, into the bargain.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XIX
TOM 
TOM TOM arrived at home in a dreary mood, and the first thing his aunt said
TOM to him showed him that he had brought his sorrows to an unpromising
TOM market:
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, I've a notion to skin you alive!''
TOM 
TOM ``Auntie, what have I done?''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, you've done enough. Here I go over to Sereny Harper, like an old
TOM softy, expecting I'm going to make her believe all that rubbage about
TOM that dream, when lo and behold you she'd found out from Joe that you was
TOM over here and heard all the talk we had that night. Tom, I don't know
TOM what is to become of a boy that will act like that. It makes me feel so
TOM bad to think you could let me go to Sereny Harper and make such a fool
TOM of myself and never say a word.''
TOM 
TOM This was a new aspect of the thing. His smartness of the morning had
TOM seemed to Tom a good joke before, and very ingenious. It merely looked
TOM mean and shabby now. He hung his head and could not think of anything to
TOM say for a moment. Then he said:
TOM 
TOM ``Auntie, I wish I hadn't done it -- but I didn't think.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, child, you never think. You never think of anything but your own
TOM selfishness. You could think to come all the way over here from
TOM Jackson's Island in the night to laugh at our troubles, and you could
TOM think to fool me with a lie about a dream; but you couldn't ever think
TOM to pity us and save us from sorrow.''
TOM 
TOM ``Auntie, I know now it was mean, but I didn't mean to be mean. I
TOM didn't, honest. And besides, I didn't come over here to laugh at you
TOM that night.''
TOM 
TOM ``What did you come for, then?''
TOM 
TOM ``It was to tell you not to be uneasy about us, because we hadn't got
TOM drownded.''
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, Tom, I would be the thankfullest soul in this world if I could
TOM believe you ever had as good a thought as that, but you know you never
TOM did -- and I know it, Tom.''
TOM 
TOM ``Indeed and 'deed I did, auntie -- I wish I may never stir if I
TOM didn't.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, Tom, don't lie -- don't do it. It only makes things a hundred
TOM times worse.''
TOM 
TOM ``It ain't a lie, auntie; it's the truth. I wanted to keep you from
TOM grieving -- that was all that made me come.''
TOM 
TOM ``I'd give the whole world to believe that -- it would cover up a power
TOM of sins, Tom. I'd 'most be glad you'd run off and acted so bad. But it
TOM ain't reasonable; because, why didn't you tell me, child?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, you see, when you got to talking about the funeral, I just got
TOM all full of the idea of our coming and hiding in the church, and I
TOM couldn't somehow bear to spoil it. So I just put the bark back in my
TOM pocket and kept mum.''
TOM 
TOM ``What bark?''
TOM 
TOM ``The bark I had wrote on to tell you we'd gone pirating. I wish, now,
TOM you'd waked up when I kissed you -- I do, honest.''
TOM 
TOM The hard lines in his aunt's face relaxed and a sudden tenderness dawned
TOM in her eyes.
TOM 
TOM ``DID you kiss me, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, yes, I did.''
TOM 
TOM ``Are you sure you did, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, yes, I did, auntie -- certain sure.''
TOM 
TOM ``What did you kiss me for, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``Because I loved you so, and you laid there moaning and I was so
TOM sorry.''
TOM 
TOM The words sounded like truth. The old lady could not hide a tremor in
TOM her voice when she said:
TOM 
TOM ``Kiss me again, Tom! -- and be off with you to school, now, and don't
TOM bother me any more.''
TOM 
TOM The moment he was gone, she ran to a closet and got out the ruin of a
TOM jacket which Tom had gone pirating in. Then she stopped, with it in her
TOM hand, and said to herself:
TOM 
TOM ``No, I don't dare. Poor boy, I reckon he's lied about it -- but it's a
TOM blessed, blessed lie, there's such a comfort come from it. I hope the
TOM Lord -- I KNOW the Lord will forgive him, because it was such
TOM good-heartedness in him to tell it. But I don't want to find out it's a
TOM lie. I won't look.''
TOM 
TOM She put the jacket away, and stood by musing a minute. Twice she put out
TOM her hand to take the garment again, and twice she refrained. Once more
TOM she ventured, and this time she fortified herself with the thought:
TOM ``It's a good lie -- it's a good lie -- I won't let it grieve me.'' So
TOM she sought the jacket pocket. A moment later she was reading Tom's piece
TOM of bark through flowing tears and saying: ``I could forgive the boy,
TOM now, if he'd committed a million sins!''
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XX
TOM 
TOM THERE was something about Aunt Polly's manner, when she kissed Tom, that
TOM swept away his low spirits and made him light-hearted and happy again.
TOM He started to school and had the luck of coming upon Becky Thatcher at
TOM the head of Meadow Lane. His mood always determined his manner. Without
TOM a moment's hesitation he ran to her and said:
TOM 
TOM ``I acted mighty mean to-day, Becky, and I'm so sorry. I won't ever,
TOM ever do that way again, as long as ever I live -- please make up, won't
TOM you?''
TOM 
TOM The girl stopped and looked him scornfully in the face:
TOM 
TOM ``I'll thank you to keep yourself TO yourself, Mr. Thomas Sawyer. I'll
TOM never speak to you again.''
TOM 
TOM She tossed her head and passed on. Tom was so stunned that he had not
TOM even presence of mind enough to say ``Who cares, Miss Smarty?'' until
TOM the right time to say it had gone by. So he said nothing. But he was in
TOM a fine rage, nevertheless. He moped into the schoolyard wishing she were
TOM a boy, and imagining how he would trounce her if she were. He presently
TOM encountered her and delivered a stinging remark as he passed. She hurled
TOM one in return, and the angry breach was complete. It seemed to Becky, in
TOM her hot resentment, that she could hardly wait for school to ``take
TOM in,'' she was so impatient to see Tom flogged for the injured
TOM spelling-book. If she had had any lingering notion of exposing Alfred
TOM Temple, Tom's offensive fling had driven it entirely away.
TOM 
TOM Poor girl, she did not know how fast she was nearing trouble herself.
TOM The master, Mr. Dobbins, had reached middle age with an unsatisfied
TOM ambition. The darling of his desires was, to be a doctor, but poverty
TOM had decreed that he should be nothing higher than a village
TOM schoolmaster. Every day he took a mysterious book out of his desk and
TOM absorbed himself in it at times when no classes were reciting. He kept
TOM that book under lock and key. There was not an urchin in school but was
TOM perishing to have a glimpse of it, but the chance never came. Every boy
TOM and girl had a theory about the nature of that book; but no two theories
TOM were alike, and there was no way of getting at the facts in the case.
TOM Now, as Becky was passing by the desk, which stood near the door, she
TOM noticed that the key was in the lock! It was a precious moment. She
TOM glanced around; found herself alone, and the next instant she had the
TOM book in her hands. The title-page -- Professor Somebody's ANATOMY --
TOM carried no information to her mind; so she began to turn the leaves. She
TOM came at once upon a handsomely engraved and colored frontispiece -- a
TOM human figure, stark naked. At that moment a shadow fell on the page and
TOM Tom Sawyer stepped in at the door and caught a glimpse of the picture.
TOM Becky snatched at the book to close it, and had the hard luck to tear
TOM the pictured page half down the middle. She thrust the volume into the
TOM desk, turned the key, and burst out crying with shame and vexation.
TOM 
TOM ``Tom Sawyer, you are just as mean as you can be, to sneak up on a
TOM person and look at what they're looking at.''
TOM 
TOM ``How could I know you was looking at anything?''
TOM 
TOM ``You ought to be ashamed of yourself, Tom Sawyer; you know you're going
TOM to tell on me, and oh, what shall I do, what shall I do! I'll be
TOM whipped, and I never was whipped in school.''
TOM 
TOM Then she stamped her little foot and said:
TOM 
TOM ``BE so mean if you want to! I know something that's going to happen.
TOM You just wait and you'll see! Hateful, hateful, hateful!'' -- and she
TOM flung out of the house with a new explosion of crying.
TOM 
TOM Tom stood still, rather flustered by this onslaught. Presently he said
TOM to himself:
TOM 
TOM ``What a curious kind of a fool a girl is! Never been licked in school!
TOM Shucks! What's a licking! That's just like a girl -- they're so
TOM thin-skinned and chicken-hearted. Well, of course I ain't going to tell
TOM old Dobbins on this little fool, because there's other ways of getting
TOM even on her, that ain't so mean; but what of it? Old Dobbins will ask
TOM who it was tore his book. Nobody'll answer. Then he'll do just the way
TOM he always does -- ask first one and then t'other, and when he comes to
TOM the right girl he'll know it, without any telling. Girls' faces always
TOM tell on them. They ain't got any backbone. She'll get licked. Well, it's
TOM a kind of a tight place for Becky Thatcher, because there ain't any way
TOM out of it.'' Tom conned the thing a moment longer, and then added: ``All
TOM right, though; she'd like to see me in just such a fix -- let her sweat
TOM it out!''
TOM 
TOM Tom joined the mob of skylarking scholars outside. In a few moments the
TOM master arrived and school ``took in.'' Tom did not feel a strong
TOM interest in his studies. Every time he stole a glance at the girls' side
TOM of the room Becky's face troubled him. Considering all things, he did
TOM not want to pity her, and yet it was all he could do to help it. He
TOM could get up no exultation that was really worthy the name. Presently
TOM the spelling-book discovery was made, and Tom's mind was entirely full
TOM of his own matters for a while after that. Becky roused up from her
TOM lethargy of distress and showed good interest in the proceedings. She
TOM did not expect that Tom could get out of his trouble by denying that he
TOM spilt the ink on the book himself; and she was right. The denial only
TOM seemed to make the thing worse for Tom. Becky supposed she would be glad
TOM of that, and she tried to believe she was glad of it, but she found she
TOM was not certain. When the worst came to the worst, she had an impulse to
TOM get up and tell on Alfred Temple, but she made an effort and forced
TOM herself to keep still -- because, said she to herself, ``he'll tell
TOM about me tearing the picture sure. I wouldn't say a word, not to save
TOM his life!''
TOM 
TOM Tom took his whipping and went back to his seat not at all
TOM broken-hearted, for he thought it was possible that he had unknowingly
TOM upset the ink on the spelling-book himself, in some skylarking bout --
TOM he had denied it for form's sake and because it was custom, and had
TOM stuck to the denial from principle.
TOM 
TOM A whole hour drifted by, the master sat nodding in his throne, the air
TOM was drowsy with the hum of study. By and by, Mr. Dobbins straightened
TOM himself up, yawned, then unlocked his desk, and reached for his book,
TOM but seemed undecided whether to take it out or leave it. Most of the
TOM pupils glanced up languidly, but there were two among them that watched
TOM his movements with intent eyes. Mr. Dobbins fingered his book absently
TOM for a while, then took it out and settled himself in his chair to read!
TOM Tom shot a glance at Becky. He had seen a hunted and helpless rabbit
TOM look as she did, with a gun levelled at its head. Instantly he forgot
TOM his quarrel with her. Quick -- something must be done! done in a flash,
TOM too! But the very imminence of the emergency paralyzed his invention.
TOM Good! -- he had an inspiration! He would run and snatch the book, spring
TOM through the door and fly. But his resolution shook for one little
TOM instant, and the chance was lost -- the master opened the volume. If Tom
TOM only had the wasted opportunity back again! Too late. There was no help
TOM for Becky now, he said. The next moment the master faced the school.
TOM Every eye sank under his gaze. There was that in it which smote even the
TOM innocent with fear. There was silence while one might count ten -- the
TOM master was gathering his wrath. Then he spoke: ``Who tore this book?''
TOM 
TOM There was not a sound. One could have heard a pin drop. The stillness
TOM continued; the master searched face after face for signs of guilt.
TOM 
TOM ``Benjamin Rogers, did you tear this book?''
TOM 
TOM A denial. Another pause.
TOM 
TOM ``Joseph Harper, did you?''
TOM 
TOM Another denial. Tom's uneasiness grew more and more intense under the
TOM slow torture of these proceedings. The master scanned the ranks of boys
TOM -- considered a while, then turned to the girls:
TOM 
TOM ``Amy Lawrence?''
TOM 
TOM A shake of the head.
TOM 
TOM ``Gracie Miller?''
TOM 
TOM The same sign.
TOM 
TOM ``Susan Harper, did you do this?''
TOM 
TOM Another negative. The next girl was Becky Thatcher. Tom was trembling
TOM from head to foot with excitement and a sense of the hopelessness of the
TOM situation.
TOM 
TOM ``Rebecca Thatcher'' [Tom glanced at her face -- it was white with
TOM terror] -- ``did you tear -- no, look me in the face'' [her hands rose
TOM in appeal] -- ``did you tear this book?''
TOM 
TOM A thought shot like lightning through Tom's brain. He sprang to his feet
TOM and shouted -- ``I done it!''
TOM 
TOM The school stared in perplexity at this incredible folly. Tom stood a
TOM moment, to gather his dismembered faculties; and when he stepped forward
TOM to go to his punishment the surprise, the gratitude, the adoration that
TOM shone upon him out of poor Becky's eyes seemed pay enough for a hundred
TOM floggings. Inspired by the splendor of his own act, he took without an
TOM outcry the most merciless flaying that even Mr. Dobbins had ever
TOM administered; and also received with indifference the added cruelty of a
TOM command to remain two hours after school should be dismissed -- for he
TOM knew who would wait for him outside till his captivity was done, and not
TOM count the tedious time as loss, either.
TOM 
TOM Tom went to bed that night planning vengeance against Alfred Temple; for
TOM with shame and repentance Becky had told him all, not forgetting her own
TOM treachery; but even the longing for vengeance had to give way, soon, to
TOM pleasanter musings, and he fell asleep at last with Becky's latest words
TOM lingering dreamily in his ear --
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, how COULD you be so noble!''
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXI
TOM 
TOM VACATION was approaching. The school-master, always severe, grew severer
TOM and more exacting than ever, for he wanted the school to make a good
TOM showing on ``Examination'' day. His rod and his ferule were seldom idle
TOM now -- at least among the smaller pupils. Only the biggest boys, and
TOM young ladies of eighteen and twenty, escaped lashing. Mr. Dobbins'
TOM lashings were very vigorous ones, too; for although he carried, under
TOM his wig, a perfectly bald and shiny head, he had only reached middle
TOM age, and there was no sign of feebleness in his muscle. As the great day
TOM approached, all the tyranny that was in him came to the surface; he
TOM seemed to take a vindictive pleasure in punishing the least
TOM shortcomings. The consequence was, that the smaller boys spent their
TOM days in terror and suffering and their nights in plotting revenge. They
TOM threw away no opportunity to do the master a mischief. But he kept ahead
TOM all the time. The retribution that followed every vengeful success was
TOM so sweeping and majestic that the boys always retired from the field
TOM badly worsted. At last they conspired together and hit upon a plan that
TOM promised a dazzling victory. They swore in the sign-painter's boy, told
TOM him the scheme, and asked his help. He had his own reasons for being
TOM delighted, for the master boarded in his father's family and had given
TOM the boy ample cause to hate him. The master's wife would go on a visit
TOM to the country in a few days, and there would be nothing to interfere
TOM with the plan; the master always prepared himself for great occasions by
TOM getting pretty well fuddled, and the sign-painter's boy said that when
TOM the dominie had reached the proper condition on Examination Evening he
TOM would ``manage the thing'' while he napped in his chair; then he would
TOM have him awakened at the right time and hurried away to school.
TOM 
TOM In the fulness of time the interesting occasion arrived. At eight in the
TOM evening the schoolhouse was brilliantly lighted, and adorned with
TOM wreaths and festoons of foliage and flowers. The master sat throned in
TOM his great chair upon a raised platform, with his blackboard behind him.
TOM He was looking tolerably mellow. Three rows of benches on each side and
TOM six rows in front of him were occupied by the dignitaries of the town
TOM and by the parents of the pupils. To his left, back of the rows of
TOM citizens, was a spacious temporary platform upon which were seated the
TOM scholars who were to take part in the exercises of the evening; rows of
TOM small boys, washed and dressed to an intolerable state of discomfort;
TOM rows of gawky big boys; snowbanks of girls and young ladies clad in lawn
TOM and muslin and conspicuously conscious of their bare arms, their
TOM grandmothers' ancient trinkets, their bits of pink and blue ribbon and
TOM the flowers in their hair. All the rest of the house was filled with
TOM non-participating scholars.
TOM 
TOM The exercises began. A very little boy stood up and sheepishly recited,
TOM ``You'd scarce expect one of my age to speak in public on the stage,''
TOM etc. -- accompanying himself with the painfully exact and spasmodic
TOM gestures which a machine might have used -- supposing the machine to be
TOM a trifle out of order. But he got through safely, though cruelly scared,
TOM and got a fine round of applause when he made his manufactured bow and
TOM retired.
TOM 
TOM A little shamefaced girl lisped, ``Mary had a little lamb,'' etc.,
TOM performed a compassion-inspiring curtsy, got her meed of applause, and
TOM sat down flushed and happy.
TOM 
TOM Tom Sawyer stepped forward with conceited confidence and soared into the
TOM unquenchable and indestructible ``Give me liberty or give me death''
TOM speech, with fine fury and frantic gesticulation, and broke down in the
TOM middle of it. A ghastly stage-fright seized him, his legs quaked under
TOM him and he was like to choke. True, he had the manifest sympathy of the
TOM house but he had the house's silence, too, which was even worse than its
TOM sympathy. The master frowned, and this completed the disaster. Tom
TOM struggled awhile and then retired, utterly defeated. There was a weak
TOM attempt at applause, but it died early.
TOM 
TOM ``The Boy Stood on the Burning Deck'' followed; also ``The Assyrian Came
TOM Down,'' and other declamatory gems. Then there were reading exercises,
TOM and a spelling fight. The meagre Latin class recited with honor. The
TOM prime feature of the evening was in order, now -- original
TOM ``compositions'' by the young ladies. Each in her turn stepped forward
TOM to the edge of the platform, cleared her throat, held up her manuscript
TOM (tied with dainty ribbon), and proceeded to read, with labored attention
TOM to ``expression'' and punctuation. The themes were the same that had
TOM been illuminated upon similar occasions by their mothers before them,
TOM their grandmothers, and doubtless all their ancestors in the female line
TOM clear back to the Crusades. ``Friendship'' was one; ``Memories of Other
TOM Days''; ``Religion in History''; ``Dream Land''; ``The Advantages of
TOM Culture''; ``Forms of Political Government Compared and Contrasted'';
TOM ``Melancholy''; ``Filial Love''; ``Heart Longings,'' etc., etc.
TOM 
TOM A prevalent feature in these compositions was a nursed and petted
TOM melancholy; another was a wasteful and opulent gush of ``fine
TOM language''; another was a tendency to lug in by the ears particularly
TOM prized words and phrases until they were worn entirely out; and a
TOM peculiarity that conspicuously marked and marred them was the inveterate
TOM and intolerable sermon that wagged its crippled tail at the end of each
TOM and every one of them. No matter what the subject might be, a
TOM brain-racking effort was made to squirm it into some aspect or other
TOM that the moral and religious mind could contemplate with edification.
TOM The glaring insincerity of these sermons was not sufficient to compass
TOM the banishment of the fashion from the schools, and it is not sufficient
TOM to-day; it never will be sufficient while the world stands, perhaps.
TOM There is no school in all our land where the young ladies do not feel
TOM obliged to close their compositions with a sermon; and you will find
TOM that the sermon of the most frivolous and the least religious girl in
TOM the school is always the longest and the most relentlessly pious. But
TOM enough of this. Homely truth is unpalatable.
TOM 
TOM Let us return to the ``Examination.'' The first composition that was
TOM read was one entitled ``Is this, then, Life?'' Perhaps the reader can
TOM endure an extract from it:
TOM 
TOM ``In the common walks of life, with what delightful emotions does the
TOM youthful mind look forward to some anticipated scene of festivity!
TOM Imagination is busy sketching rose-tinted pictures of joy. In fancy, the
TOM voluptuous votary of fashion sees herself amid the festive throng, 'the
TOM observed of all observers.' Her graceful form, arrayed in snowy robes,
TOM is whirling through the mazes of the joyous dance; her eye is brightest,
TOM her step is lightest in the gay assembly.''
TOM 
TOM ``In such delicious fancies time quickly glides by, and the welcome hour
TOM arrives for her entrance into the Elysian world, of which she has had
TOM such bright dreams. How fairy-like does everything appear to her
TOM enchanted vision! Each new scene is more charming than the last. But
TOM after a while she finds that beneath this goodly exterior, all is
TOM vanity, the flattery which once charmed her soul, now grates harshly
TOM upon her ear; the ball-room has lost its charms; and with wasted health
TOM and imbittered heart, she turns away with the conviction that earthly
TOM pleasures cannot satisfy the longings of the soul!''
TOM 
TOM And so forth and so on. There was a buzz of gratification from time to
TOM time during the reading, accompanied by whispered ejaculations of ``How
TOM sweet!'' ``How eloquent!'' ``So true!'' etc., and after the thing had
TOM closed with a peculiarly afflicting sermon the applause was
TOM enthusiastic.
TOM 
TOM Then arose a slim, melancholy girl, whose face had the ``interesting''
TOM paleness that comes of pills and indigestion, and read a ``poem.'' Two
TOM stanzas of it will do:
TOM 
TOM ``A MISSOURI MAIDEN'S FAREWELL TO ALABAMA''
TOM 
TOM ``Alabama, good-bye! I love thee well! But yet for a while do I leave
TOM thee now! Sad, yes, sad thoughts of thee my heart doth swell, And
TOM burning recollections throng my brow! For I have wandered through thy
TOM flowery woods; Have roamed and read near Tallapoosa's stream; Have
TOM listened to Tallassee's warring floods, And wooed on Coosa's side
TOM Aurora's beam.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yet shame I not to bear an o'er-full heart, Nor blush to turn behind
TOM my tearful eyes; 'Tis from no stranger land I now must part, 'Tis to no
TOM strangers left I yield these sighs. Welcome and home were mine within
TOM this State, Whose vales I leave -- whose spires fade fast from me And
TOM cold must be mine eyes, and heart, and tete, When, dear Alabama! they
TOM turn cold on thee!''
TOM 
TOM There were very few there who knew what ``tete'' meant, but the poem was
TOM very satisfactory, nevertheless.
TOM 
TOM Next appeared a dark-complexioned, black-eyed, black-haired young lady,
TOM who paused an impressive moment, assumed a tragic expression, and began
TOM to read in a measured, solemn tone:
TOM 
TOM ``A VISION''
TOM 
TOM ``Dark and tempestuous was night. Around the throne on high not a single
TOM star quivered; but the deep intonations of the heavy thunder constantly
TOM vibrated upon the ear; whilst the terrific lightning revelled in angry
TOM mood through the cloudy chambers of heaven, seeming to scorn the power
TOM exerted over its terror by the illustrious Franklin! Even the boisterous
TOM winds unanimously came forth from their mystic homes, and blustered
TOM about as if to enhance by their aid the wildness of the scene.''
TOM 
TOM ``At such a time,so dark,so dreary, for human sympathy my very spirit
TOM sighed; but instead thereof,''
TOM 
TOM ``'My dearest friend, my counsellor, my comforter and guide -- My joy in
TOM grief, my second bliss in joy,' came to my side. She moved like one of
TOM those bright beings pictured in the sunny walks of fancy's Eden by the
TOM romantic and young, a queen of beauty unadorned save by her own
TOM transcendent loveliness. So soft was her step, it failed to make even a
TOM sound, and but for the magical thrill imparted by her genial touch, as
TOM other unobtrusive beauties, she would have glided away un-perceived --
TOM unsought. A strange sadness rested upon her features, like icy tears
TOM upon the robe of December, as she pointed to the contending elements
TOM without, and bade me contemplate the two beings presented.''
TOM 
TOM This nightmare occupied some ten pages of manuscript and wound up with a
TOM sermon so destructive of all hope to non-Presbyterians that it took the
TOM first prize. This composition was considered to be the very finest
TOM effort of the evening. The mayor of the village, in delivering the prize
TOM to the author of it, made a warm speech in which he said that it was by
TOM far the most ``eloquent'' thing he had ever listened to, and that Daniel
TOM Webster himself might well be proud of it.
TOM 
TOM It may be remarked, in passing, that the number of compositions in which
TOM the word ``beauteous'' was over-fondled, and human experience referred
TOM to as ``life's page,'' was up to the usual average.
TOM 
TOM Now the master, mellow almost to the verge of geniality, put his chair
TOM aside, turned his back to the audience, and began to draw a map of
TOM America on the blackboard, to exercise the geography class upon. But he
TOM made a sad business of it with his unsteady hand, and a smothered titter
TOM rippled over the house. He knew what the matter was, and set himself to
TOM right it. He sponged out lines and remade them; but he only distorted
TOM them more than ever, and the tittering was more pronounced. He threw his
TOM entire attention upon his work, now, as if determined not to be put down
TOM by the mirth. He felt that all eyes were fastened upon him; he imagined
TOM he was succeeding, and yet the tittering continued; it even manifestly
TOM increased. And well it might. There was a garret above, pierced with a
TOM scuttle over his head; and down through this scuttle came a cat,
TOM suspended around the haunches by a string; she had a rag tied about her
TOM head and jaws to keep her from mewing; as she slowly descended she
TOM curved upward and clawed at the string, she swung downward and clawed at
TOM the intangible air. The tittering rose higher and higher -- the cat was
TOM within six inches of the absorbed teacher's head -- down, down, a little
TOM lower, and she grabbed his wig with her desperate claws, clung to it,
TOM and was snatched up into the garret in an instant with her trophy still
TOM in her possession! And how the light did blaze abroad from the master's
TOM bald pate -- for the sign-painter's boy had GILDED it!
TOM 
TOM That broke up the meeting. The boys were avenged. Vacation had come.
TOM 
TOM NOTE:-- The pretended ``compositions'' quoted in this chapter are taken
TOM without alteration from a volume entitled ``Prose and Poetry, by a
TOM Western Lady'' -- but they are exactly and precisely after the
TOM schoolgirl pattern, and hence are much happier than any mere imitations
TOM could be.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXII
TOM 
TOM TOM joined the new order of Cadets of Temperance, being attracted by the
TOM showy character of their ``regalia.'' He promised to abstain from
TOM smoking, chewing, and profanity as long as he remained a member. Now he
TOM found out a new thing -- namely, that to promise not to do a thing is
TOM the surest way in the world to make a body want to go and do that very
TOM thing. Tom soon found himself tormented with a desire to drink and
TOM swear; the desire grew to be so intense that nothing but the hope of a
TOM chance to display himself in his red sash kept him from withdrawing from
TOM the order. Fourth of July was coming; but he soon gave that up -- gave
TOM it up before he had worn his shackles over forty-eight hours -- and
TOM fixed his hopes upon old Judge Frazer, justice of the peace, who was
TOM apparently on his deathbed and would have a big public funeral, since he
TOM was so high an official. During three days Tom was deeply concerned
TOM about the Judge's condition and hungry for news of it. Sometimes his
TOM hopes ran high -- so high that he would venture to get out his regalia
TOM and practise before the looking-glass. But the Judge had a most
TOM discouraging way of fluctuating. At last he was pronounced upon the mend
TOM -- and then convalescent. Tom was disgusted; and felt a sense of injury,
TOM too. He handed in his resignation at once -- and that night the Judge
TOM suffered a relapse and died. Tom resolved that he would never trust a
TOM man like that again.
TOM 
TOM The funeral was a fine thing. The Cadets paraded in a style calculated
TOM to kill the late member with envy. Tom was a free boy again, however --
TOM there was something in that. He could drink and swear, now -- but found
TOM to his surprise that he did not want to. The simple fact that he could,
TOM took the desire away, and the charm of it.
TOM 
TOM Tom presently wondered to find that his coveted vacation was beginning
TOM to hang a little heavily on his hands.
TOM 
TOM He attempted a diary -- but nothing happened during three days, and so
TOM he abandoned it.
TOM 
TOM The first of all the negro minstrel shows came to town, and made a
TOM sensation. Tom and Joe Harper got up a band of performers and were happy
TOM for two days.
TOM 
TOM Even the Glorious Fourth was in some sense a failure, for it rained
TOM hard, there was no procession in consequence, and the greatest man in
TOM the world (as Tom supposed), Mr. Benton, an actual United States
TOM Senator, proved an overwhelming disappointment -- for he was not
TOM twenty-five feet high, nor even anywhere in the neighborhood of it.
TOM 
TOM A circus came. The boys played circus for three days afterward in tents
TOM made of rag carpeting -- admission, three pins for boys, two for girls
TOM -- and then circusing was abandoned.
TOM 
TOM A phrenologist and a mesmerizer came -- and went again and left the
TOM village duller and drearier than ever.
TOM 
TOM There were some boys-and-girls' parties, but they were so few and so
TOM delightful that they only made the aching voids between ache the harder.
TOM 
TOM Becky Thatcher was gone to her Constantinople home to stay with her
TOM parents during vacation -- so there was no bright side to life anywhere.
TOM 
TOM The dreadful secret of the murder was a chronic misery. It was a very
TOM cancer for permanency and pain.
TOM 
TOM Then came the measles.
TOM 
TOM During two long weeks Tom lay a prisoner, dead to the world and its
TOM happenings. He was very ill, he was interested in nothing. When he got
TOM upon his feet at last and moved feebly down-town, a melancholy change
TOM had come over everything and every creature. There had been a
TOM ``revival,'' and everybody had ``got religion,'' not only the adults,
TOM but even the boys and girls. Tom went about, hoping against hope for the
TOM sight of one blessed sinful face, but disappointment crossed him
TOM everywhere. He found Joe Harper studying a Testament, and turned sadly
TOM away from the depressing spectacle. He sought Ben Rogers, and found him
TOM visiting the poor with a basket of tracts. He hunted up Jim Hollis, who
TOM called his attention to the precious blessing of his late measles as a
TOM warning. Every boy he encountered added another ton to his depression;
TOM and when, in desperation, he flew for refuge at last to the bosom of
TOM Huckleberry Finn and was received with a Scriptural quotation, his heart
TOM broke and he crept home and to bed realizing that he alone of all the
TOM town was lost, forever and forever.
TOM 
TOM And that night there came on a terrific storm, with driving rain, awful
TOM claps of thunder and blinding sheets of lightning. He covered his head
TOM with the bedclothes and waited in a horror of suspense for his doom; for
TOM he had not the shadow of a doubt that all this hubbub was about him. He
TOM believed he had taxed the forbearance of the powers above to the
TOM extremity of endurance and that this was the result. It might have
TOM seemed to him a waste of pomp and ammunition to kill a bug with a
TOM battery of artillery, but there seemed nothing incongruous about the
TOM getting up such an expensive thunder-storm as this to knock the turf
TOM from under an insect like himself.
TOM 
TOM By and by the tempest spent itself and died without accomplishing its
TOM object. The boy's first impulse was to be grateful, and reform. His
TOM second was to wait -- for there might not be any more storms.
TOM 
TOM The next day the doctors were back; Tom had relapsed. The three weeks he
TOM spent on his back this time seemed an entire age. When he got abroad at
TOM last he was hardly grateful that he had been spared, remembering how
TOM lonely was his estate, how companionless and forlorn he was. He drifted
TOM listlessly down the street and found Jim Hollis acting as judge in a
TOM juvenile court that was trying a cat for murder, in the presence of her
TOM victim, a bird. He found Joe Harper and Huck Finn up an alley eating a
TOM stolen melon. Poor lads! they -- like Tom -- had suffered a relapse.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXIII
TOM 
TOM AT last the sleepy atmosphere was stirred -- and vigorously: the murder
TOM trial came on in the court. It became the absorbing topic of village
TOM talk immediately. Tom could not get away from it. Every reference to the
TOM murder sent a shudder to his heart, for his troubled conscience and
TOM fears almost persuaded him that these remarks were put forth in his
TOM hearing as ``feelers''; he did not see how he could be suspected of
TOM knowing anything about the murder, but still he could not be comfortable
TOM in the midst of this gossip. It kept him in a cold shiver all the time.
TOM He took Huck to a lonely place to have a talk with him. It would be some
TOM relief to unseal his tongue for a little while; to divide his burden of
TOM distress with another sufferer. Moreover, he wanted to assure himself
TOM that Huck had remained discreet.
TOM 
TOM ``Huck, have you ever told anybody about -- that?''
TOM 
TOM ``'Bout what?''
TOM 
TOM ``You know what.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh -- 'course I haven't.''
TOM 
TOM ``Never a word?''
TOM 
TOM ``Never a solitary word, so help me. What makes you ask?''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I was afeard.''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, Tom Sawyer, we wouldn't be alive two days if that got found out.
TOM YOU know that.''
TOM 
TOM Tom felt more comfortable. After a pause:
TOM 
TOM ``Huck, they couldn't anybody get you to tell, could they?''
TOM 
TOM ``Get me to tell? Why, if I wanted that half-breed devil to drownd me
TOM they could get me to tell. They ain't no different way.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, that's all right, then. I reckon we're safe as long as we keep
TOM mum. But let's swear again, anyway. It's more surer.''
TOM 
TOM ``I'm agreed.''
TOM 
TOM So they swore again with dread solemnities.
TOM 
TOM ``What is the talk around, Huck? I've heard a power of it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Talk? Well, it's just Muff Potter, Muff Potter, Muff Potter all the
TOM time. It keeps me in a sweat, constant, so's I want to hide som'ers.''
TOM 
TOM ``That's just the same way they go on round me. I reckon he's a goner.
TOM Don't you feel sorry for him, sometimes?''
TOM 
TOM ``Most always -- most always. He ain't no account; but then he hain't
TOM ever done anything to hurt anybody. Just fishes a little, to get money
TOM to get drunk on -- and loafs around considerable; but lord, we all do
TOM that -- leastways most of us -- preachers and such like. But he's kind
TOM of good -- he give me half a fish, once, when there warn't enough for
TOM two; and lots of times he's kind of stood by me when I was out of
TOM luck.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, he's mended kites for me, Huck, and knitted hooks on to my line.
TOM I wish we could get him out of there.''
TOM 
TOM ``My! we couldn't get him out, Tom. And besides, 'twouldn't do any good;
TOM they'd ketch him again.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes -- so they would. But I hate to hear 'em abuse him so like the
TOM dickens when he never done -- that.''
TOM 
TOM ``I do too, Tom. Lord, I hear 'em say he's the bloodiest looking villain
TOM in this country, and they wonder he wasn't ever hung before.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, they talk like that, all the time. I've heard 'em say that if he
TOM was to get free they'd lynch him.''
TOM 
TOM ``And they'd do it, too.''
TOM 
TOM The boys had a long talk, but it brought them little comfort. As the
TOM twilight drew on, they found themselves hanging about the neighborhood
TOM of the little isolated jail, perhaps with an undefined hope that
TOM something would happen that might clear away their difficulties. But
TOM nothing happened; there seemed to be no angels or fairies interested in
TOM this luckless captive.
TOM 
TOM The boys did as they had often done before -- went to the cell grating
TOM and gave Potter some tobacco and matches. He was on the ground floor and
TOM there were no guards.
TOM 
TOM His gratitude for their gifts had always smote their consciences before
TOM -- it cut deeper than ever, this time. They felt cowardly and
TOM treacherous to the last degree when Potter said:
TOM 
TOM ``You've been mighty good to me, boys -- better'n anybody else in this
TOM town. And I don't forget it, I don't. Often I says to myself, says I, 'I
TOM used to mend all the boys' kites and things, and show 'em where the good
TOM fishin' places was, and befriend 'em what I could, and now they've all
TOM forgot old Muff when he's in trouble; but Tom don't, and Huck don't --
TOM THEY don't forget him, says I, 'and I don't forget them.' Well, boys, I
TOM done an awful thing -- drunk and crazy at the time -- that's the only
TOM way I account for it -- and now I got to swing for it, and it's right.
TOM Right, and BEST, too, I reckon -- hope so, anyway. Well, we won't talk
TOM about that. I don't want to make YOU feel bad; you've befriended me. But
TOM what I want to say, is, don't YOU ever get drunk -- then you won't ever
TOM get here. Stand a litter furder west -- so -- that's it; it's a prime
TOM comfort to see faces that's friendly when a body's in such a muck of
TOM trouble, and there don't none come here but yourn. Good friendly faces
TOM -- good friendly faces. Git up on one another's backs and let me touch
TOM 'em. That's it. Shake hands -- yourn'll come through the bars, but
TOM mine's too big. Little hands, and weak -- but they've helped Muff Potter
TOM a power, and they'd help him more if they could.''
TOM 
TOM Tom went home miserable, and his dreams that night were full of horrors.
TOM The next day and the day after, he hung about the court-room, drawn by
TOM an almost irresistible impulse to go in, but forcing himself to stay
TOM out. Huck was having the same experience. They studiously avoided each
TOM other. Each wandered away, from time to time, but the same dismal
TOM fascination always brought them back presently. Tom kept his ears open
TOM when idlers sauntered out of the court-room, but invariably heard
TOM distressing news -- the toils were closing more and more relentlessly
TOM around poor Potter. At the end of the second day the village talk was to
TOM the effect that Injun Joe's evidence stood firm and unshaken, and that
TOM there was not the slightest question as to what the jury's verdict would
TOM be.
TOM 
TOM Tom was out late, that night, and came to bed through the window. He was
TOM in a tremendous state of excitement. It was hours before he got to
TOM sleep. All the village flocked to the court-house the next morning, for
TOM this was to be the great day. Both sexes were about equally represented
TOM in the packed audience. After a long wait the jury filed in and took
TOM their places; shortly afterward, Potter, pale and haggard, timid and
TOM hopeless, was brought in, with chains upon him, and seated where all the
TOM curious eyes could stare at him; no less conspicuous was Injun Joe,
TOM stolid as ever. There was another pause, and then the judge arrived and
TOM the sheriff proclaimed the opening of the court. The usual whisperings
TOM among the lawyers and gathering together of papers followed. These
TOM details and accompanying delays worked up an atmosphere of preparation
TOM that was as impressive as it was fascinating.
TOM 
TOM Now a witness was called who testified that he found Muff Potter washing
TOM in the brook, at an early hour of the morning that the murder was
TOM discovered, and that he immediately sneaked away. After some further
TOM questioning, counsel for the prosecution said:
TOM 
TOM ``Take the witness.''
TOM 
TOM The prisoner raised his eyes for a moment, but dropped them again when
TOM his own counsel said:
TOM 
TOM ``I have no questions to ask him.''
TOM 
TOM The next witness proved the finding of the knife near the corpse.
TOM Counsel for the prosecution said:
TOM 
TOM ``Take the witness.''
TOM 
TOM ``I have no questions to ask him,'' Potter's lawyer replied.
TOM 
TOM A third witness swore he had often seen the knife in Potter's
TOM possession.
TOM 
TOM ``Take the witness.''
TOM 
TOM Counsel for Potter declined to question him. The faces of the audience
TOM began to betray annoyance. Did this attorney mean to throw away his
TOM client's life without an effort?
TOM 
TOM Several witnesses deposed concerning Potter's guilty behavior when
TOM brought to the scene of the murder. They were allowed to leave the stand
TOM without being cross-questioned.
TOM 
TOM Every detail of the damaging circumstances that occurred in the
TOM graveyard upon that morning which all present remembered so well was
TOM brought out by credible witnesses, but none of them were cross-examined
TOM by Potter's lawyer. The perplexity and dissatisfaction of the house
TOM expressed itself in murmurs and provoked a reproof from the bench.
TOM Counsel for the prosecution now said:
TOM 
TOM ``By the oaths of citizens whose simple word is above suspicion, we have
TOM fastened this awful crime, beyond all possibility of question, upon the
TOM unhappy prisoner at the bar. We rest our case here.''
TOM 
TOM A groan escaped from poor Potter, and he put his face in his hands and
TOM rocked his body softly to and fro, while a painful silence reigned in
TOM the court-room. Many men were moved, and many women's compassion
TOM testified itself in tears. Counsel for the defence rose and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Your honor, in our remarks at the opening of this trial, we
TOM foreshadowed our purpose to prove that our client did this fearful deed
TOM while under the influence of a blind and irresponsible delirium produced
TOM by drink. We have changed our mind. We shall not offer that plea.''
TOM [Then to the clerk:] ``Call Thomas Sawyer!''
TOM 
TOM A puzzled amazement awoke in every face in the house, not even excepting
TOM Potter's. Every eye fastened itself with wondering interest upon Tom as
TOM he rose and took his place upon the stand. The boy looked wild enough,
TOM for he was badly scared. The oath was administered.
TOM 
TOM ``Thomas Sawyer, where were you on the seventeenth of June, about the
TOM hour of midnight?''
TOM 
TOM Tom glanced at Injun Joe's iron face and his tongue failed him. The
TOM audience listened breathless, but the words refused to come. After a few
TOM moments, however, the boy got a little of his strength back, and managed
TOM to put enough of it into his voice to make part of the house hear:
TOM 
TOM ``In the graveyard!''
TOM 
TOM ``A little bit louder, please. Don't be afraid. You were --''
TOM 
TOM ``In the graveyard.''
TOM 
TOM A contemptuous smile flitted across Injun Joe's face.
TOM 
TOM ``Were you anywhere near Horse Williams' grave?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, sir.''
TOM 
TOM ``Speak up -- just a trifle louder. How near were you?''
TOM 
TOM ``Near as I am to you.''
TOM 
TOM ``Were you hidden, or not?''
TOM 
TOM ``I was hid.''
TOM 
TOM ``Where?''
TOM 
TOM ``Behind the elms that's on the edge of the grave.''
TOM 
TOM Injun Joe gave a barely perceptible start.
TOM 
TOM ``Any one with you?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, sir. I went there with --''
TOM 
TOM ``Wait -- wait a moment. Never mind mentioning your companion's name. We
TOM will produce him at the proper time. Did you carry anything there with
TOM you.''
TOM 
TOM Tom hesitated and looked confused.
TOM 
TOM ``Speak out, my boy -- don't be diffident. The truth is always
TOM respectable. What did you take there?''
TOM 
TOM ``Only a -- a -- dead cat.''
TOM 
TOM There was a ripple of mirth, which the court checked.
TOM 
TOM ``We will produce the skeleton of that cat. Now, my boy, tell us
TOM everything that occurred -- tell it in your own way -- don't skip
TOM anything, and don't be afraid.''
TOM 
TOM Tom began -- hesitatingly at first, but as he warmed to his subject his
TOM words flowed more and more easily; in a little while every sound ceased
TOM but his own voice; every eye fixed itself upon him; with parted lips and
TOM bated breath the audience hung upon his words, taking no note of time,
TOM rapt in the ghastly fascinations of the tale. The strain upon pent
TOM emotion reached its climax when the boy said:
TOM 
TOM ``-- and as the doctor fetched the board around and Muff Potter fell,
TOM Injun Joe jumped with the knife and --''
TOM 
TOM Crash! Quick as lightning the half-breed sprang for a window, tore his
TOM way through all opposers, and was gone!
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXIV
TOM 
TOM TOM was a glittering hero once more -- the pet of the old, the envy of
TOM the young. His name even went into immortal print, for the village paper
TOM magnified him. There were some that believed he would be President, yet,
TOM if he escaped hanging.
TOM 
TOM As usual, the fickle, unreasoning world took Muff Potter to its bosom
TOM and fondled him as lavishly as it had abused him before. But that sort
TOM of conduct is to the world's credit; therefore it is not well to find
TOM fault with it.
TOM 
TOM Tom's days were days of splendor and exultation to him, but his nights
TOM were seasons of horror. Injun Joe infested all his dreams, and always
TOM with doom in his eye. Hardly any temptation could persuade the boy to
TOM stir abroad after nightfall. Poor Huck was in the same state of
TOM wretchedness and terror, for Tom had told the whole story to the lawyer
TOM the night before the great day of the trial, and Huck was sore afraid
TOM that his share in the business might leak out, yet, notwithstanding
TOM Injun Joe's flight had saved him the suffering of testifying in court.
TOM The poor fellow had got the attorney to promise secrecy, but what of
TOM that? Since Tom's harassed conscience had managed to drive him to the
TOM lawyer's house by night and wring a dread tale from lips that had been
TOM sealed with the dismalest and most formidable of oaths, Huck's
TOM confidence in the human race was well-nigh obliterated.
TOM 
TOM Daily Muff Potter's gratitude made Tom glad he had spoken; but nightly
TOM he wished he had sealed up his tongue.
TOM 
TOM Half the time Tom was afraid Injun Joe would never be captured; the
TOM other half he was afraid he would be. He felt sure he never could draw a
TOM safe breath again until that man was dead and he had seen the corpse.
TOM 
TOM Rewards had been offered, the country had been scoured, but no Injun Joe
TOM was found. One of those omniscient and awe-inspiring marvels, a
TOM detective, came up from St. Louis, moused around, shook his head, looked
TOM wise, and made that sort of astounding success which members of that
TOM craft usually achieve. That is to say, he ``found a clew.'' But you
TOM can't hang a ``clew'' for murder, and so after that detective had got
TOM through and gone home, Tom felt just as insecure as he was before.
TOM 
TOM The slow days drifted on, and each left behind it a slightly lightened
TOM weight of apprehension.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXV
TOM 
TOM THERE comes a time in every rightly-constructed boy's life when he has a
TOM raging desire to go somewhere and dig for hidden treasure. This desire
TOM suddenly came upon Tom one day. He sallied out to find Joe Harper, but
TOM failed of success. Next he sought Ben Rogers; he had gone fishing.
TOM Presently he stumbled upon Huck Finn the Red-Handed. Huck would answer.
TOM Tom took him to a private place and opened the matter to him
TOM confidentially. Huck was willing. Huck was always willing to take a hand
TOM in any enterprise that offered entertainment and required no capital,
TOM for he had a troublesome superabundance of that sort of time which is
TOM not money. ``Where'll we dig?'' said Huck.
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, most anywhere.''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, is it hid all around?''
TOM 
TOM ``No, indeed it ain't. It's hid in mighty particular places, Huck --
TOM sometimes on islands, sometimes in rotten chests under the end of a limb
TOM of an old dead tree, just where the shadow falls at midnight; but mostly
TOM under the floor in ha'nted houses.''
TOM 
TOM ``Who hides it?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, robbers, of course -- who'd you reckon? Sunday-school
TOM sup'rintendents?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't know. If 'twas mine I wouldn't hide it; I'd spend it and have
TOM a good time.''
TOM 
TOM ``So would I. But robbers don't do that way. They always hide it and
TOM leave it there.''
TOM 
TOM ``Don't they come after it any more?''
TOM 
TOM ``No, they think they will, but they generally forget the marks, or else
TOM they die. Anyway, it lays there a long time and gets rusty; and by and
TOM by somebody finds an old yellow paper that tells how to find the marks
TOM -- a paper that's got to be ciphered over about a week because it's
TOM mostly signs and hy'roglyphics.''
TOM 
TOM ``HyroQwhich?''
TOM 
TOM ``Hy'roglyphics -- pictures and things, you know, that don't seem to
TOM mean anything.''
TOM 
TOM ``Have you got one of them papers, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``No.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well then, how you going to find the marks?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't want any marks. They always bury it under a ha'nted house or
TOM on an island, or under a dead tree that's got one limb sticking out.
TOM Well, we've tried Jackson's Island a little, and we can try it again
TOM some time; and there's the old ha'nted house up the Still-House branch,
TOM and there's lots of deadlimb trees -- dead loads of 'em.''
TOM 
TOM ``Is it under all of them?''
TOM 
TOM ``How you talk! No!''
TOM 
TOM ``Then how you going to know which one to go for?''
TOM 
TOM ``Go for all of 'em!''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, Tom, it'll take all summer.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, what of that? Suppose you find a brass pot with a hundred
TOM dollars in it, all rusty and gray, or rotten chest full of di'monds.
TOM How's that?''
TOM 
TOM Huck's eyes glowed.
TOM 
TOM ``That's bully. Plenty bully enough for me. Just you gimme the hundred
TOM dollars and I don't want no di'monds.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right. But I bet you I ain't going to throw off on di'monds. Some
TOM of 'em's worth twenty dollars apiece -- there ain't any, hardly, but's
TOM worth six bits or a dollar.''
TOM 
TOM ``No! Is that so?''
TOM 
TOM ``Cert'nly -- anybody'll tell you so. Hain't you ever seen one, Huck?''
TOM 
TOM ``Not as I remember.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, kings have slathers of them.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I don' know no kings, Tom.''
TOM 
TOM ``I reckon you don't. But if you was to go to Europe you'd see a raft of
TOM 'em hopping around.''
TOM 
TOM ``Do they hop?''
TOM 
TOM ``Hop? -- your granny! No!''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, what did you say they did, for?''
TOM 
TOM ``Shucks, I only meant you'd SEE 'em -- not hopping, of course -- what
TOM do they want to hop for? -- but I mean you'd just see 'em -- scattered
TOM around, you know, in a kind of a general way. Like that old humpbacked
TOM Richard.''
TOM 
TOM ``Richard? What's his other name?''
TOM 
TOM ``He didn't have any other name. Kings don't have any but a given
TOM name.''
TOM 
TOM ``No?''
TOM 
TOM ``But they don't.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, if they like it, Tom, all right; but I don't want to be a king
TOM and have only just a given name, like a nigger. But say -- where you
TOM going to dig first?''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I don't know. S'pose we tackle that old dead-limb tree on the
TOM hill t'other side of Still-House branch?''
TOM 
TOM ``I'm agreed.''
TOM 
TOM So they got a crippled pick and a shovel, and set out on their
TOM three-mile tramp. They arrived hot and panting, and threw themselves
TOM down in the shade of a neighboring elm to rest and have a smoke.
TOM 
TOM ``I like this,'' said Tom.
TOM 
TOM ``So do I.''
TOM 
TOM ``Say, Huck, if we find a treasure here, what you going to do with your
TOM share?''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I'll have pie and a glass of soda every day, and I'll go to
TOM every circus that comes along. I bet I'll have a gay time.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, ain't you going to save any of it?''
TOM 
TOM ``Save it? What for?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, so as to have something to live on, by and by.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, that ain't any use. Pap would come back to thish-yer town some day
TOM and get his claws on it if I didn't hurry up, and I tell you he'd clean
TOM it out pretty quick. What you going to do with yourn, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``I'm going to buy a new drum, and a sure-'nough sword, and a red
TOM necktie and a bull pup, and get married.''
TOM 
TOM ``Married!''
TOM 
TOM ``That's it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, you -- why, you ain't in your right mind.''
TOM 
TOM ``Wait -- you'll see.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, that's the foolishest thing you could do. Look at pap and my
TOM mother. Fight! Why, they used to fight all the time. I remember, mighty
TOM well.''
TOM 
TOM ``That ain't anything. The girl I'm going to marry won't fight.''
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, I reckon they're all alike. They'll all comb a body. Now you
TOM better think 'bout this awhile. I tell you you better. What's the name
TOM of the gal?''
TOM 
TOM ``It ain't a gal at all -- it's a girl.''
TOM 
TOM ``It's all the same, I reckon; some says gal, some says girl -- both's
TOM right, like enough. Anyway, what's her name, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``I'll tell you some time -- not now.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right -- that'll do. Only if you get married I'll be more
TOM lonesomer than ever.''
TOM 
TOM ``No you won't. You'll come and live with me. Now stir out of this and
TOM we'll go to digging.''
TOM 
TOM They worked and sweated for half an hour. No result. They toiled another
TOM half-hour. Still no result. Huck said:
TOM 
TOM ``Do they always bury it as deep as this?''
TOM 
TOM ``Sometimes -- not always. Not generally. I reckon we haven't got the
TOM right place.''
TOM 
TOM So they chose a new spot and began again. The labor dragged a little,
TOM but still they made progress. They pegged away in silence for some time.
TOM Finally Huck leaned on his shovel, swabbed the beaded drops from his
TOM brow with his sleeve, and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Where you going to dig next, after we get this one?''
TOM 
TOM ``I reckon maybe we'll tackle the old tree that's over yonder on Cardiff
TOM Hill back of the widow's.''
TOM 
TOM ``I reckon that'll be a good one. But won't the widow take it away from
TOM us, Tom? It's on her land.''
TOM 
TOM ``SHE take it away! Maybe she'd like to try it once. Whoever finds one
TOM of these hid treasures, it belongs to him. It don't make any difference
TOM whose land it's on.''
TOM 
TOM That was satisfactory. The work went on. By and by Huck said:
TOM 
TOM ``Blame it, we must be in the wrong place again. What do you think?''
TOM 
TOM ``It is mighty curious, Huck. I don't understand it. Sometimes witches
TOM interfere. I reckon maybe that's what's the trouble now.''
TOM 
TOM ``Shucks! Witches ain't got no power in the daytime.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, that's so. I didn't think of that. Oh, I know what the matter
TOM is! What a blamed lot of fools we are! You got to find out where the
TOM shadow of the limb falls at midnight, and that's where you dig!''
TOM 
TOM ``Then consound it, we've fooled away all this work for nothing. Now
TOM hang it all, we got to come back in the night. It's an awful long way.
TOM Can you get out?''
TOM 
TOM ``I bet I will. We've got to do it to-night, too, because if somebody
TOM sees these holes they'll know in a minute what's here and they'll go for
TOM it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I'll come around and maow to-night.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right. Let's hide the tools in the bushes.''
TOM 
TOM The boys were there that night, about the appointed time. They sat in
TOM the shadow waiting. It was a lonely place, and an hour made solemn by
TOM old traditions. Spirits whispered in the rustling leaves, ghosts lurked
TOM in the murky nooks, the deep baying of a hound floated up out of the
TOM distance, an owl answered with his sepulchral note. The boys were
TOM subdued by these solemnities, and talked little. By and by they judged
TOM that twelve had come; they marked where the shadow fell, and began to
TOM dig. Their hopes commenced to rise. Their interest grew stronger, and
TOM their industry kept pace with it. The hole deepened and still deepened,
TOM but every time their hearts jumped to hear the pick strike upon
TOM something, they only suffered a new disappointment. It was only a stone
TOM or a chunk. At last Tom said:
TOM 
TOM ``It ain't any use, Huck, we're wrong again.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, but we CAN'T be wrong. We spotted the shadder to a dot.''
TOM 
TOM ``I know it, but then there's another thing.''
TOM 
TOM ``What's that?''.
TOM 
TOM ``Why, we only guessed at the time. Like enough it was too late or too
TOM early.''
TOM 
TOM Huck dropped his shovel.
TOM 
TOM ``That's it,'' said he. ``That's the very trouble. We got to give this
TOM one up. We can't ever tell the right time, and besides this kind of
TOM thing's too awful, here this time of night with witches and ghosts
TOM a-fluttering around so. I feel as if something's behind me all the time;
TOM and I'm afeard to turn around, becuz maybe there's others in front
TOM a-waiting for a chance. I been creeping all over, ever since I got
TOM here.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I've been pretty much so, too, Huck. They most always put in a
TOM dead man when they bury a treasure under a tree, to look out for it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Lordy!''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, they do. I've always heard that.''
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, I don't like to fool around much where there's dead people. A
TOM body's bound to get into trouble with 'em, sure.''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't like to stir 'em up, either. S'pose this one here was to stick
TOM his skull out and say something!''
TOM 
TOM ``Don't Tom! It's awful.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, it just is. Huck, I don't feel comfortable a bit.''
TOM 
TOM ``Say, Tom, let's give this place up, and try somewheres else.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right, I reckon we better.''
TOM 
TOM ``What'll it be?''
TOM 
TOM Tom considered awhile; and then said:
TOM 
TOM ``The ha'nted house. That's it!''
TOM 
TOM ``Blame it, I don't like ha'nted houses, Tom. Why, they're a dern sight
TOM worse'n dead people. Dead people might talk, maybe, but they don't come
TOM sliding around in a shroud, when you ain't noticing, and peep over your
TOM shoulder all of a sudden and grit their teeth, the way a ghost does. I
TOM couldn't stand such a thing as that, Tom -- nobody could.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, but, Huck, ghosts don't travel around only at night. They won't
TOM hender us from digging there in the daytime.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, that's so. But you know mighty well people don't go about that
TOM ha'nted house in the day nor the night.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, that's mostly because they don't like to go where a man's been
TOM murdered, anyway -- but nothing's ever been seen around that house
TOM except in the night -- just some blue lights slipping by the windows --
TOM no regular ghosts.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, where you see one of them blue lights flickering around, Tom,
TOM you can bet there's a ghost mighty close behind it. It stands to reason.
TOM Becuz you know that they don't anybody but ghosts use 'em.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, that's so. But anyway they don't come around in the daytime, so
TOM what's the use of our being afeard?''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, all right. We'll tackle the ha'nted house if you say so -- but I
TOM reckon it's taking chances.''
TOM 
TOM They had started down the hill by this time. There in the middle of the
TOM moonlit valley below them stood the ``ha'nted'' house, utterly isolated,
TOM its fences gone long ago, rank weeds smothering the very doorsteps, the
TOM chimney crumbled to ruin, the window-sashes vacant, a corner of the roof
TOM caved in. The boys gazed awhile, half expecting to see a blue light flit
TOM past a window; then talking in a low tone, as befitted the time and the
TOM circumstances, they struck far off to the right, to give the haunted
TOM house a wide berth, and took their way homeward through the woods that
TOM adorned the rearward side of Cardiff Hill.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXVI
TOM 
TOM ABOUT noon the next day the boys arrived at the dead tree; they had come
TOM for their tools. Tom was impatient to go to the haunted house; Huck was
TOM measurably so, also -- but suddenly said:
TOM 
TOM ``Lookyhere, Tom, do you know what day it is?''
TOM 
TOM Tom mentally ran over the days of the week, and then quickly lifted his
TOM eyes with a startled look in them --
TOM 
TOM ``My! I never once thought of it, Huck!''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I didn't neither, but all at once it popped onto me that it was
TOM Friday.''
TOM 
TOM ``Blame it, a body can't be too careful, Huck. We might 'a' got into an
TOM awful scrape, tackling such a thing on a Friday.''
TOM 
TOM ``MIGHT! Better say we WOULD! There's some lucky days, maybe, but Friday
TOM ain't.''
TOM 
TOM ``Any fool knows that. I don't reckon YOU was the first that found it
TOM out, Huck.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I never said I was, did I? And Friday ain't all, neither. I had
TOM a rotten bad dream last night -- dreampt about rats.''
TOM 
TOM ``No! Sure sign of trouble. Did they fight?''
TOM 
TOM ``No.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, that's good, Huck. When they don't fight it's only a sign that
TOM there's trouble around, you know. All we got to do is to look mighty
TOM sharp and keep out of it. We'll drop this thing for to-day, and play. Do
TOM you know Robin Hood, Huck?''
TOM 
TOM ``No. Who's Robin Hood?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, he was one of the greatest men that was ever in England -- and
TOM the best. He was a robber.''
TOM 
TOM ``Cracky, I wisht I was. Who did he rob?''
TOM 
TOM ``Only sheriffs and bishops and rich people and kings, and such like.
TOM But he never bothered the poor. He loved 'em. He always divided up with
TOM 'em perfectly square.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, he must 'a' been a brick.''
TOM 
TOM ``I bet you he was, Huck. Oh, he was the noblest man that ever was. They
TOM ain't any such men now, I can tell you. He could lick any man in
TOM England, with one hand tied behind him; and he could take his yew bow
TOM and plug a ten-cent piece every time, a mile and a half.''
TOM 
TOM ``What's a YEW bow?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't know. It's some kind of a bow, of course. And if he hit that
TOM dime only on the edge he would set down and cry -- and curse. But we'll
TOM play Robin Hood -- it's nobby fun. I'll learn you.''
TOM 
TOM ``I'm agreed.''
TOM 
TOM So they played Robin Hood all the afternoon, now and then casting a
TOM yearning eye down upon the haunted house and passing a remark about the
TOM morrow's prospects and possibilities there. As the sun began to sink
TOM into the west they took their way homeward athwart the long shadows of
TOM the trees and soon were buried from sight in the forests of Cardiff
TOM Hill.
TOM 
TOM On Saturday, shortly after noon, the boys were at the dead tree again.
TOM They had a smoke and a chat in the shade, and then dug a little in their
TOM last hole, not with great hope, but merely because Tom said there were
TOM so many cases where people had given up a treasure after getting down
TOM within six inches of it, and then somebody else had come along and
TOM turned it up with a single thrust of a shovel. The thing failed this
TOM time, however, so the boys shouldered their tools and went away feeling
TOM that they had not trifled with fortune, but had fulfilled all the
TOM requirements that belong to the business of treasure-hunting.
TOM 
TOM When they reached the haunted house there was something so weird and
TOM grisly about the dead silence that reigned there under the baking sun,
TOM and something so depressing about the loneliness and desolation of the
TOM place, that they were afraid, for a moment, to venture in. Then they
TOM crept to the door and took a trembling peep. They saw a weed-grown,
TOM floorless room, unplastered, an ancient fireplace, vacant windows, a
TOM ruinous staircase; and here, there, and everywhere hung ragged and
TOM abandoned cobwebs. They presently entered, softly, with quickened
TOM pulses, talking in whispers, ears alert to catch the slightest sound,
TOM and muscles tense and ready for instant retreat.
TOM 
TOM In a little while familiarity modified their fears and they gave the
TOM place a critical and interested examination, rather admiring their own
TOM boldness, and wondering at it, too. Next they wanted to look up-stairs.
TOM This was something like cutting off retreat, but they got to daring each
TOM other, and of course there could be but one result -- they threw their
TOM tools into a corner and made the ascent. Up there were the same signs of
TOM decay. In one corner they found a closet that promised mystery, but the
TOM promise was a fraud -- there was nothing in it. Their courage was up now
TOM and well in hand. They were about to go down and begin work when --
TOM 
TOM ``Sh!'' said Tom.
TOM 
TOM ``What is it?'' whispered Huck, blanching with fright.
TOM 
TOM ``Sh! ... There! ... Hear it?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes! ... Oh, my! Let's run!''
TOM 
TOM ``Keep still! Don't you budge! They're coming right toward the door.''
TOM 
TOM The boys stretched themselves upon the floor with their eyes to
TOM knot-holes in the planking, and lay waiting, in a misery of fear.
TOM 
TOM ``They've stopped.... No -- coming.... Here they are. Don't whisper
TOM another word, Huck. My goodness, I wish I was out of this!''
TOM 
TOM Two men entered. Each boy said to himself: ``There's the old deaf and
TOM dumb Spaniard that's been about town once or twice lately -- never saw
TOM t'other man before.''
TOM 
TOM ``T'other'' was a ragged, unkempt creature, with nothing very pleasant
TOM in his face. The Spaniard was wrapped in a serape; he had bushy white
TOM whiskers; long white hair flowed from under his sombrero, and he wore
TOM green goggles. When they came in, ``t'other'' was talking in a low
TOM voice; they sat down on the ground, facing the door, with their backs to
TOM the wall, and the speaker continued his remarks. His manner became less
TOM guarded and his words more distinct as he proceeded:
TOM 
TOM ``No,'' said he, ``I've thought it all over, and I don't like it. It's
TOM dangerous.''
TOM 
TOM ``Dangerous!'' grunted the ``deaf and dumb'' Spaniard -- to the vast
TOM surprise of the boys. ``Milksop!''
TOM 
TOM This voice made the boys gasp and quake. It was Injun Joe's! There was
TOM silence for some time. Then Joe said:
TOM 
TOM ``What's any more dangerous than that job up yonder -- but nothing's
TOM come of it.''
TOM 
TOM ``That's different. Away up the river so, and not another house about.
TOM 'Twon't ever be known that we tried, anyway, long as we didn't
TOM succeed.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, what's more dangerous than coming here in the daytime! --
TOM anybody would suspicion us that saw us.''
TOM 
TOM ``I know that. But there warn't any other place as handy after that fool
TOM of a job. I want to quit this shanty. I wanted to yesterday, only it
TOM warn't any use trying to stir out of here, with those infernal boys
TOM playing over there on the hill right in full view.''
TOM 
TOM ``Those infernal boys'' quaked again under the inspiration of this
TOM remark, and thought how lucky it was that they had remembered it was
TOM Friday and concluded to wait a day. They wished in their hearts they had
TOM waited a year.
TOM 
TOM The two men got out some food and made a luncheon. After a long and
TOM thoughtful silence, Injun Joe said:
TOM 
TOM ``Look here, lad -- you go back up the river where you belong. Wait
TOM there till you hear from me. I'll take the chances on dropping into this
TOM town just once more, for a look. We'll do that 'dangerous' job after
TOM I've spied around a little and think things look well for it. Then for
TOM Texas! We'll leg it together!''
TOM 
TOM This was satisfactory. Both men presently fell to yawning, and Injun Joe
TOM said:
TOM 
TOM ``I'm dead for sleep! It's your turn to watch.''
TOM 
TOM He curled down in the weeds and soon began to snore. His comrade stirred
TOM him once or twice and he became quiet. Presently the watcher began to
TOM nod; his head drooped lower and lower, both men began to snore now.
TOM 
TOM The boys drew a long, grateful breath. Tom whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``Now's our chance -- come!''
TOM 
TOM Huck said:
TOM 
TOM ``I can't -- I'd die if they was to wake.''
TOM 
TOM Tom urged -- Huck held back. At last Tom rose slowly and softly, and
TOM started alone. But the first step he made wrung such a hideous creak
TOM from the crazy floor that he sank down almost dead with fright. He never
TOM made a second attempt. The boys lay there counting the dragging moments
TOM till it seemed to them that time must be done and eternity growing gray;
TOM and then they were grateful to note that at last the sun was setting.
TOM 
TOM Now one snore ceased. Injun Joe sat up, stared around -- smiled grimly
TOM upon his comrade, whose head was drooping upon his knees -- stirred him
TOM up with his foot and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Here! YOU'RE a watchman, ain't you! All right, though -- nothing's
TOM happened.''
TOM 
TOM ``My! have I been asleep?''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, partly, partly. Nearly time for us to be moving, pard. What'll we
TOM do with what little swag we've got left?''
TOM 
TOM ``I don't know -- leave it here as we've always done, I reckon. No use
TOM to take it away till we start south. Six hundred and fifty in silver's
TOM something to carry.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well -- all right -- it won't matter to come here once more.''
TOM 
TOM ``No -- but I'd say come in the night as we used to do -- it's better.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes: but look here; it may be a good while before I get the right
TOM chance at that job; accidents might happen; 'tain't in such a very good
TOM place; we'll just regularly bury it -- and bury it deep.''
TOM 
TOM ``Good idea,'' said the comrade, who walked across the room, knelt down,
TOM raised one of the rearward hearth-stones and took out a bag that jingled
TOM pleasantly. He subtracted from it twenty or thirty dollars for himself
TOM and as much for Injun Joe, and passed the bag to the latter, who was on
TOM his knees in the corner, now, digging with his bowie-knife.
TOM 
TOM The boys forgot all their fears, all their miseries in an instant. With
TOM gloating eyes they watched every movement. Luck! -- the splendor of it
TOM was beyond all imagination! Six hundred dollars was money enough to make
TOM half a dozen boys rich! Here was treasurehunting under the happiest
TOM auspices -- there would not be any bothersome uncertainty as to where to
TOM dig. They nudged each other every moment -- eloquent nudges and easily
TOM understood, for they simply meant -- ``Oh, but ain't you glad NOW we're
TOM here!''
TOM 
TOM Joe's knife struck upon something.
TOM 
TOM ``Hello!'' said he.
TOM 
TOM ``What is it?'' said his comrade.
TOM 
TOM ``Half-rotten plank -- no, it's a box, I believe. Here -- bear a hand
TOM and we'll see what it's here for. Never mind, I've broke a hole.''
TOM 
TOM He reached his hand in and drew it out --
TOM 
TOM ``Man, it's money!''
TOM 
TOM The two men examined the handful of coins. They were gold. The boys
TOM above were as excited as themselves, and as delighted.
TOM 
TOM Joe's comrade said:
TOM 
TOM ``We'll make quick work of this. There's an old rusty pick over amongst
TOM the weeds in the corner the other side of the fireplace -- I saw it a
TOM minute ago.''
TOM 
TOM He ran and brought the boys' pick and shovel. Injun Joe took the pick,
TOM looked it over critically, shook his head, muttered something to
TOM himself, and then began to use it. The box was soon unearthed. It was
TOM not very large; it was iron bound and had been very strong before the
TOM slow years had injured it. The men contemplated the treasure awhile in
TOM blissful silence.
TOM 
TOM ``Pard, there's thousands of dollars here,'' said Injun Joe.
TOM 
TOM ``'Twas always said that Murrel's gang used to be around here one
TOM summer,'' the stranger observed.
TOM 
TOM ``I know it,'' said Injun Joe; ``and this looks like it, I should say.''
TOM 
TOM ``Now you won't need to do that job.''
TOM 
TOM The half-breed frowned. Said he:
TOM 
TOM ``You don't know me. Least you don't know all about that thing. 'Tain't
TOM robbery altogether -- it's REVENGE!'' and a wicked light flamed in his
TOM eyes. ``I'll need your help in it. When it's finished -- then Texas. Go
TOM home to your Nance and your kids, and stand by till you hear from me.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well -- if you say so; what'll we do with this -- bury it again?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes. [Ravishing delight overhead.] NO! by the great Sachem, no!
TOM [Profound distress overhead.] I'd nearly forgot. That pick had fresh
TOM earth on it! [The boys were sick with terror in a moment.] What business
TOM has a pick and a shovel here? What business with fresh earth on them?
TOM Who brought them here -- and where are they gone? Have you heard
TOM anybody? -- seen anybody? What! bury it again and leave them to come and
TOM see the ground disturbed? Not exactly -- not exactly. We'll take it to
TOM my den.''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, of course! Might have thought of that before. You mean Number
TOM One?''
TOM 
TOM ``No -- Number Two -- under the cross. The other place is bad -- too
TOM common.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right. It's nearly dark enough to start.''
TOM 
TOM Injun Joe got up and went about from window to window cautiously peeping
TOM out. Presently he said:
TOM 
TOM ``Who could have brought those tools here? Do you reckon they can be
TOM up-stairs?''
TOM 
TOM The boys' breath forsook them. Injun Joe put his hand on his knife,
TOM halted a moment, undecided, and then turned toward the stairway. The
TOM boys thought of the closet, but their strength was gone. The steps came
TOM creaking up the stairs -- the intolerable distress of the situation woke
TOM the stricken resolution of the lads -- they were about to spring for the
TOM closet, when there was a crash of rotten timbers and Injun Joe landed on
TOM the ground amid the debris of the ruined stairway. He gathered himself
TOM up cursing, and his comrade said:
TOM 
TOM ``Now what's the use of all that? If it's anybody, and they're up there,
TOM let them STAY there -- who cares? If they want to jump down, now, and
TOM get into trouble, who objects? It will be dark in fifteen minutes -- and
TOM then let them follow us if they want to. I'm willing. In my opinion,
TOM whoever hove those things in here caught a sight of us and took us for
TOM ghosts or devils or something. I'll bet they're running yet.''
TOM 
TOM Joe grumbled awhile; then he agreed with his friend that what daylight
TOM was left ought to be economized in getting things ready for leaving.
TOM Shortly afterward they slipped out of the house in the deepening
TOM twilight, and moved toward the river with their precious box.
TOM 
TOM Tom and Huck rose up, weak but vastly relieved, and stared after them
TOM through the chinks between the logs of the house. Follow? Not they. They
TOM were content to reach ground again without broken necks, and take the
TOM townward track over the hill. They did not talk much. They were too much
TOM absorbed in hating themselves -- hating the ill luck that made them take
TOM the spade and the pick there. But for that, Injun Joe never would have
TOM suspected. He would have hidden the silver with the gold to wait there
TOM till his ``revenge'' was satisfied, and then he would have had the
TOM misfortune to find that money turn up missing. Bitter, bitter luck that
TOM the tools were ever brought there!
TOM 
TOM They resolved to keep a lookout for that Spaniard when he should come to
TOM town spying out for chances to do his revengeful job, and follow him to
TOM ``Number Two,'' wherever that might be. Then a ghastly thought occurred
TOM to Tom.
TOM 
TOM ``Revenge? What if he means US, Huck!''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, don't!'' said Huck, nearly fainting.
TOM 
TOM They talked it all over, and as they entered town they agreed to believe
TOM that he might possibly mean somebody else -- at least that he might at
TOM least mean nobody but Tom, since only Tom had testified.
TOM 
TOM Very, very small comfort it was to Tom to be alone in danger! Company
TOM would be a palpable improvement, he thought.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXVII
TOM 
TOM THE adventure of the day mightily tormented Tom's dreams that night.
TOM Four times he had his hands on that rich treasure and four times it
TOM wasted to nothingness in his fingers as sleep forsook him and
TOM wakefulness brought back the hard reality of his misfortune. As he lay
TOM in the early morning recalling the incidents of his great adventure, he
TOM noticed that they seemed curiously subdued and far away -- somewhat as
TOM if they had happened in another world, or in a time long gone by. Then
TOM it occurred to him that the great adventure itself must be a dream!
