TOM the lead. They toiled their way to the farther end of the tunnel, then
TOM made their spliced kite-strings fast and moved on. A few steps brought
TOM them to the spring, and Tom felt a shudder quiver all through him. He
TOM showed Huck the fragment of candle-wick perched on a lump of clay
TOM against the wall, and described how he and Becky had watched the flame
TOM struggle and expire.
TOM 
TOM The boys began to quiet down to whispers, now, for the stillness and
TOM gloom of the place oppressed their spirits. They went on, and presently
TOM entered and followed Tom's other corridor until they reached the
TOM ``jumping-off place.'' The candles revealed the fact that it was not
TOM really a precipice, but only a steep clay hill twenty or thirty feet
TOM high. Tom whispered:
TOM 
TOM ``Now I'll show you something, Huck.''
TOM 
TOM He held his candle aloft and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Look as far around the corner as you can. Do you see that? There -- on
TOM the big rock over yonder -- done with candle-smoke.''
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, it's a CROSS!''
TOM 
TOM ``NOW where's your Number Two? 'UNDER THE CROSS,' hey? Right yonder's
TOM where I saw Injun Joe poke up his candle, Huck!''
TOM 
TOM Huck stared at the mystic sign awhile, and then said with a shaky voice:
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, less git out of here!''
TOM 
TOM ``What! and leave the treasure?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes -- leave it. Injun Joe's ghost is round about there, certain.''
TOM 
TOM ``No it ain't, Huck, no it ain't. It would ha'nt the place where he died
TOM -- away out at the mouth of the cave -- five mile from here.''
TOM 
TOM ``No, Tom, it wouldn't. It would hang round the money. I know the ways
TOM of ghosts, and so do you.''
TOM 
TOM Tom began to fear that Huck was right. Misgivings gathered in his mind.
TOM But presently an idea occurred to him --
TOM 
TOM ``Lookyhere, Huck, what fools we're making of ourselves! Injun Joe's
TOM ghost ain't a going to come around where there's a cross!''
TOM 
TOM The point was well taken. It had its effect.
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, I didn't think of that. But that's so. It's luck for us, that
TOM cross is. I reckon we'll climb down there and have a hunt for that
TOM box.''
TOM 
TOM Tom went first, cutting rude steps in the clay hill as he descended.
TOM Huck followed. Four avenues opened out of the small cavern which the
TOM great rock stood in. The boys examined three of them with no result.
TOM They found a small recess in the one nearest the base of the rock, with
TOM a pallet of blankets spread down in it; also an old suspender, some
TOM bacon rind, and the well-gnawed bones of two or three fowls. But there
TOM was no money-box. The lads searched and researched this place, but in
TOM vain. Tom said:
TOM 
TOM ``He said UNDER the cross. Well, this comes nearest to being under the
TOM cross. It can't be under the rock itself, because that sets solid on the
TOM ground.''
TOM 
TOM They searched everywhere once more, and then sat down discouraged. Huck
TOM could suggest nothing. By-and-by Tom said:
TOM 
TOM ``Lookyhere, Huck, there's footprints and some candle-grease on the clay
TOM about one side of this rock, but not on the other sides. Now, what's
TOM that for? I bet you the money IS under the rock. I'm going to dig in the
TOM clay.''
TOM 
TOM ``That ain't no bad notion, Tom!'' said Huck with animation.
TOM 
TOM Tom's ``real Barlow'' was out at once, and he had not dug four inches
TOM before he struck wood.
TOM 
TOM ``Hey, Huck! -- you hear that?''
TOM 
TOM Huck began to dig and scratch now. Some boards were soon uncovered and
TOM removed. They had concealed a natural chasm which led under the rock.
TOM Tom got into this and held his candle as far under the rock as he could,
TOM but said he could not see to the end of the rift. He proposed to
TOM explore. He stooped and passed under; the narrow way descended
TOM gradually. He followed its winding course, first to the right, then to
TOM the left, Huck at his heels. Tom turned a short curve, by-and-by, and
TOM exclaimed:
TOM 
TOM ``My goodness, Huck, lookyhere!''
TOM 
TOM It was the treasure-box, sure enough, occupying a snug little cavern,
TOM along with an empty powder-keg, a couple of guns in leather cases, two
TOM or three pairs of old moccasins, a leather belt, and some other rubbish
TOM well soaked with the water-drip.
TOM 
TOM ``Got it at last!'' said Huck, ploughing among the tarnished coins with
TOM his hand. ``My, but we're rich, Tom!''
TOM 
TOM ``Huck, I always reckoned we'd get it. It's just too good to believe,
TOM but we HAVE got it, sure! Say -- let's not fool around here. Let's snake
TOM it out. Lemme see if I can lift the box.''
TOM 
TOM It weighed about fifty pounds. Tom could lift it, after an awkward
TOM fashion, but could not carry it conveniently.
TOM 
TOM ``I thought so,'' he said; ``THEY carried it like it was heavy, that day
TOM at the ha'nted house. I noticed that. I reckon I was right to think of
TOM fetching the little bags along.''
TOM 
TOM The money was soon in the bags and the boys took it up to the cross
TOM rock.
TOM 
TOM ``Now less fetch the guns and things,'' said Huck.
TOM 
TOM ``No, Huck -- leave them there. They're just the tricks to have when we
TOM go to robbing. We'll keep them there all the time, and we'll hold our
TOM orgies there, too. It's an awful snug place for orgies.''
TOM 
TOM ``What orgies?''
TOM 
TOM ``I dono. But robbers always have orgies, and of course we've got to
TOM have them, too. Come along, Huck, we've been in here a long time. It's
TOM getting late, I reckon. I'm hungry, too. We'll eat and smoke when we get
TOM to the skiff.''
TOM 
TOM They presently emerged into the clump of sumach bushes, looked warily
TOM out, found the coast clear, and were soon lunching and smoking in the
TOM skiff. As the sun dipped toward the horizon they pushed out and got
TOM under way. Tom skimmed up the shore through the long twilight, chatting
TOM cheerily with Huck, and landed shortly after dark.
TOM 
TOM ``Now, Huck,'' said Tom, ``we'll hide the money in the loft of the
TOM widow's woodshed, and I'll come up in the morning and we'll count it and
TOM divide, and then we'll hunt up a place out in the woods for it where it
TOM will be safe. Just you lay quiet here and watch the stuff till I run and
TOM hook Benny Taylor's little wagon; I won't be gone a minute.''
TOM 
TOM He disappeared, and presently returned with the wagon, put the two small
TOM sacks into it, threw some old rags on top of them, and started off,
TOM dragging his cargo behind him. When the boys reached the Welshman's
TOM house, they stopped to rest. Just as they were about to move on, the
TOM Welshman stepped out and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Hallo, who's that?''
TOM 
TOM ``Huck and Tom Sawyer.''
TOM 
TOM ``Good! Come along with me, boys, you are keeping everybody waiting.
TOM Here -- hurry up, trot ahead -- I'll haul the wagon for you. Why, it's
TOM not as light as it might be. Got bricks in it? -- or old metal?''
TOM 
TOM ``Old metal,'' said Tom.
TOM 
TOM ``I judged so; the boys in this town will take more trouble and fool
TOM away more time hunting up six bits' worth of old iron to sell to the
TOM foundry than they would to make twice the money at regular work. But
TOM that's human nature -- hurry along, hurry along!''
TOM 
TOM The boys wanted to know what the hurry was about.
TOM 
TOM ``Never mind; you'll see, when we get to the Widow Douglas'.''
TOM 
TOM Huck said with some apprehension -- for he was long used to being
TOM falsely accused:
TOM 
TOM ``Mr. Jones, we haven't been doing nothing.''
TOM 
TOM The Welshman laughed.
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I don't know, Huck, my boy. I don't know about that. Ain't you
TOM and the widow good friends?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes. Well, she's ben good friends to me, anyway.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right, then. What do you want to be afraid for?''
TOM 
TOM This question was not entirely answered in Huck's slow mind before he
TOM found himself pushed, along with Tom, into Mrs. Douglas' drawing-room.
TOM Mr. Jones left the wagon near the door and followed.
TOM 
TOM The place was grandly lighted, and everybody that was of any consequence
TOM in the village was there. The Thatchers were there, the Harpers, the
TOM Rogerses, Aunt Polly, Sid, Mary, the minister, the editor, and a great
TOM many more, and all dressed in their best. The widow received the boys as
TOM heartily as any one could well receive two such looking beings. They
TOM were covered with clay and candle-grease. Aunt Polly blushed crimson
TOM with humiliation, and frowned and shook her head at Tom. Nobody suffered
TOM half as much as the two boys did, however. Mr. Jones said:
TOM 
TOM ``Tom wasn't at home, yet, so I gave him up; but I stumbled on him and
TOM Huck right at my door, and so I just brought them along in a hurry.''
TOM 
TOM ``And you did just right,'' said the widow. ``Come with me, boys.''
TOM 
TOM She took them to a bedchamber and said:
TOM 
TOM ``Now wash and dress yourselves. Here are two new suits of clothes --
TOM shirts, socks, everything complete. They're Huck's -- no, no thanks,
TOM Huck -- Mr. Jones bought one and I the other. But they'll fit both of
TOM you. Get into them. We'll wait -- come down when you are slicked up
TOM enough.''
TOM 
TOM Then she left.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXXIV
TOM 
TOM HUCK said: ``Tom, we can slope, if we can find a rope. The window ain't
TOM high from the ground.''
TOM 
TOM ``Shucks! what do you want to slope for?''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I ain't used to that kind of a crowd. I can't stand it. I ain't
TOM going down there, Tom.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, bother! It ain't anything. I don't mind it a bit. I'll take care
TOM of you.''
TOM 
TOM Sid appeared.
TOM 
TOM ``Tom,'' said he, ``auntie has been waiting for you all the afternoon.
TOM Mary got your Sunday clothes ready, and everybody's been fretting about
TOM you. Say -- ain't this grease and clay, on your clothes?''
TOM 
TOM ``Now, Mr. Siddy, you jist 'tend to your own business. What's all this
TOM blow-out about, anyway?''
TOM 
TOM ``It's one of the widow's parties that she's always having. This time
TOM it's for the Welshman and his sons, on account of that scrape they
TOM helped her out of the other night. And say -- I can tell you something,
TOM if you want to know.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, what?''
TOM 
TOM ``Why, old Mr. Jones is going to try to spring something on the people
TOM here to-night, but I overheard him tell auntie to-day about it, as a
TOM secret, but I reckon it's not much of a secret now. Everybody knows --
TOM the widow, too, for all she tries to let on she don't. Mr. Jones was
TOM bound Huck should be here -- couldn't get along with his grand secret
TOM without Huck, you know!''
TOM 
TOM ``Secret about what, Sid?''
TOM 
TOM ``About Huck tracking the robbers to the widow's. I reckon Mr. Jones was
TOM going to make a grand time over his surprise, but I bet you it will drop
TOM pretty flat.''
TOM 
TOM Sid chuckled in a very contented and satisfied way.
TOM 
TOM ``Sid, was it you that told?''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, never mind who it was. SOMEBODY told -- that's enough.''
TOM 
TOM ``Sid, there's only one person in this town mean enough to do that, and
TOM that's you. If you had been in Huck's place you'd 'a' sneaked down the
TOM hill and never told anybody on the robbers. You can't do any but mean
TOM things, and you can't bear to see anybody praised for doing good ones.
TOM There -- no thanks, as the widow says'' -- and Tom cuffed Sid's ears and
TOM helped him to the door with several kicks. ``Now go and tell auntie if
TOM you dare -- and to-morrow you'll catch it!''
TOM 
TOM Some minutes later the widow's guests were at the supper-table, and a
TOM dozen children were propped up at little side-tables in the same room,
TOM after the fashion of that country and that day. At the proper time Mr.
TOM Jones made his little speech, in which he thanked the widow for the
TOM honor she was doing himself and his sons, but said that there was
TOM another person whose modesty --
TOM 
TOM And so forth and so on. He sprung his secret about Huck's share in the
TOM adventure in the finest dramatic manner he was master of, but the
TOM surprise it occasioned was largely counterfeit and not as clamorous and
TOM effusive as it might have been under happier circumstances. However, the
TOM widow made a pretty fair show of astonishment, and heaped so many
TOM compliments and so much gratitude upon Huck that he almost forgot the
TOM nearly intolerable discomfort of his new clothes in the entirely
TOM intolerable discomfort of being set up as a target for everybody's gaze
TOM and everybody's laudations.
TOM 
TOM The widow said she meant to give Huck a home under her roof and have him
TOM educated; and that when she could spare the money she would start him in
TOM business in a modest way. Tom's chance was come. He said:
TOM 
TOM ``Huck don't need it. Huck's rich.''
TOM 
TOM Nothing but a heavy strain upon the good manners of the company kept
TOM back the due and proper complimentary laugh at this pleasant joke. But
TOM the silence was a little awkward. Tom broke it:
TOM 
TOM ``Huck's got money. Maybe you don't believe it, but he's got lots of it.
TOM Oh, you needn't smile -- I reckon I can show you. You just wait a
TOM minute.''
TOM 
TOM Tom ran out of doors. The company looked at each other with a perplexed
TOM interest -- and inquiringly at Huck, who was tongue-tied.
TOM 
TOM ``Sid, what ails Tom?'' said Aunt Polly. ``He -- well, there ain't ever
TOM any making of that boy out. I never --''
TOM 
TOM Tom entered, struggling with the weight of his sacks, and Aunt Polly did
TOM not finish her sentence. Tom poured the mass of yellow coin upon the
TOM table and said:
TOM 
TOM ``There -- what did I tell you? Half of it's Huck's and half of it's
TOM mine!''
TOM 
TOM The spectacle took the general breath away. All gazed, nobody spoke for
TOM a moment. Then there was a unanimous call for an explanation. Tom said
TOM he could furnish it, and he did. The tale was long, but brimful of
TOM interest. There was scarcely an interruption from any one to break the
TOM charm of its flow. When he had finished, Mr. Jones said:
TOM 
TOM ``I thought I had fixed up a little surprise for this occasion, but it
TOM don't amount to anything now. This one makes it sing mighty small, I'm
TOM willing to allow.''
TOM 
TOM The money was counted. The sum amounted to a little over twelve thousand
TOM dollars. It was more than any one present had ever seen at one time
TOM before, though several persons were there who were worth considerably
TOM more than that in property.
TOM 
TOM 
TOM Chapter XXXV
TOM 
TOM THE reader may rest satisfied that Tom's and Huck's windfall made a
TOM mighty stir in the poor little village of St. Petersburg. So vast a sum,
TOM all in actual cash, seemed next to incredible. It was talked about,
TOM gloated over, glorified, until the reason of many of the citizens
TOM tottered under the strain of the unhealthy excitement. Every ``haunted''
TOM house in St. Petersburg and the neighboring villages was dissected,
TOM plank by plank, and its foundations dug up and ransacked for hidden
TOM treasure -- and not by boys, but men -- pretty grave, unromantic men,
TOM too, some of them. Wherever Tom and Huck appeared they were courted,
TOM admired, stared at. The boys were not able to remember that their
TOM remarks had possessed weight before; but now their sayings were
TOM treasured and repeated; everything they did seemed somehow to be
TOM regarded as remarkable; they had evidently lost the power of doing and
TOM saying commonplace things; moreover, their past history was raked up and
TOM discovered to bear marks of conspicuous originality. The village paper
TOM published biographical sketches of the boys.
TOM 
TOM The Widow Douglas put Huck's money out at six per cent., and Judge
TOM Thatcher did the same with Tom's at Aunt Polly's request. Each lad had
TOM an income, now, that was simply prodigious -- a dollar for every
TOM week-day in the year and half of the Sundays. It was just what the
TOM minister got -- no, it was what he was promised -- he generally couldn't
TOM collect it. A dollar and a quarter a week would board, lodge, and school
TOM a boy in those old simple days -- and clothe him and wash him, too, for
TOM that matter.
TOM 
TOM Judge Thatcher had conceived a great opinion of Tom. He said that no
TOM commonplace boy would ever have got his daughter out of the cave. When
TOM Becky told her father, in strict confidence, how Tom had taken her
TOM whipping at school, the Judge was visibly moved; and when she pleaded
TOM grace for the mighty lie which Tom had told in order to shift that
TOM whipping from her shoulders to his own, the Judge said with a fine
TOM outburst that it was a noble, a generous, a magnanimous lie -- a lie
TOM that was worthy to hold up its head and march down through history
TOM breast to breast with George Washington's lauded Truth about the
TOM hatchet! Becky thought her father had never looked so tall and so superb
TOM as when he walked the floor and stamped his foot and said that. She went
TOM straight off and told Tom about it.
TOM 
TOM Judge Thatcher hoped to see Tom a great lawyer or a great soldier some
TOM day. He said he meant to look to it that Tom should be admitted to the
TOM National Military Academy and afterward trained in the best law school
TOM in the country, in order that he might be ready for either career or
TOM both.
TOM 
TOM Huck Finn's wealth and the fact that he was now under the Widow Douglas'
TOM protection introduced him into society -- no, dragged him into it,
TOM hurled him into it -- and his sufferings were almost more than he could
TOM bear. The widow's servants kept him clean and neat, combed and brushed,
TOM and they bedded him nightly in unsympathetic sheets that had not one
TOM little spot or stain which he could press to his heart and know for a
TOM friend. He had to eat with a knife and fork; he had to use napkin, cup,
TOM and plate; he had to learn his book, he had to go to church; he had to
TOM talk so properly that speech was become insipid in his mouth;
TOM whithersoever he turned, the bars and shackles of civilization shut him
TOM in and bound him hand and foot.
TOM 
TOM He bravely bore his miseries three weeks, and then one day turned up
TOM missing. For forty-eight hours the widow hunted for him everywhere in
TOM great distress. The public were profoundly concerned; they searched high
TOM and low, they dragged the river for his body. Early the third morning
TOM Tom Sawyer wisely went poking among some old empty hogsheads down behind
TOM the abandoned slaughter-house, and in one of them he found the refugee.
TOM Huck had slept there; he had just breakfasted upon some stolen odds and
TOM ends of food, and was lying off, now, in comfort, with his pipe. He was
TOM unkempt, uncombed, and clad in the same old ruin of rags that had made
TOM him picturesque in the days when he was free and happy. Tom routed him
TOM out, told him the trouble he had been causing, and urged him to go home.
TOM Huck's face lost its tranquil content, and took a melancholy cast. He
TOM said:
TOM 
TOM ``Don't talk about it, Tom. I've tried it, and it don't work; it don't
TOM work, Tom. It ain't for me; I ain't used to it. The widder's good to me,
TOM and friendly; but I can't stand them ways. She makes me get up just at
TOM the same time every morning; she makes me wash, they comb me all to
TOM thunder; she won't let me sleep in the woodshed; I got to wear them
TOM blamed clothes that just smothers me, Tom; they don't seem to any air
TOM git through 'em, somehow; and they're so rotten nice that I can't set
TOM down, nor lay down, nor roll around anywher's; I hain't slid on a
TOM cellar-door for -- well, it 'pears to be years; I got to go to church
TOM and sweat and sweat -- I hate them ornery sermons! I can't ketch a fly
TOM in there, I can't chaw. I got to wear shoes all Sunday. The widder eats
TOM by a bell; she goes to bed by a bell; she gits up by a bell --
TOM everything's so awful reg'lar a body can't stand it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, everybody does that way, Huck.''
TOM 
TOM ``Tom, it don't make no difference. I ain't everybody, and I can't STAND
TOM it. It's awful to be tied up so. And grub comes too easy -- I don't take
TOM no interest in vittles, that way. I got to ask to go a-fishing; I got to
TOM ask to go in a-swimming -- dern'd if I hain't got to ask to do
TOM everything. Well, I'd got to talk so nice it wasn't no comfort -- I'd
TOM got to go up in the attic and rip out awhile, every day, to git a taste
TOM in my mouth, or I'd a died, Tom. The widder wouldn't let me smoke; she
TOM wouldn't let me yell, she wouldn't let me gape, nor stretch, nor
TOM scratch, before folks --'' [Then with a spasm of special irritation and
TOM injury] -- ``And dad fetch it, she prayed all the time! I never see such
TOM a woman! I HAD to shove, Tom -- I just had to. And besides, that
TOM school's going to open, and I'd a had to go to it -- well, I wouldn't
TOM stand THAT, Tom. Looky-here, Tom, being rich ain't what it's cracked up
TOM to be. It's just worry and worry, and sweat and sweat, and a-wishing you
TOM was dead all the time. Now these clothes suits me, and this bar'l suits
TOM me, and I ain't ever going to shake 'em any more. Tom, I wouldn't ever
TOM got into all this trouble if it hadn't 'a' ben for that money; now you
TOM just take my sheer of it along with your'n, and gimme a ten-center
TOM sometimes -- not many times, becuz I don't give a dern for a thing
TOM 'thout it's tollable hard to git -- and you go and beg off for me with
TOM the widder.''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, Huck, you know I can't do that. 'Tain't fair; and besides if
TOM you'll try this thing just a while longer you'll come to like it.''
TOM 
TOM ``Like it! Yes -- the way I'd like a hot stove if I was to set on it
TOM long enough. No, Tom, I won't be rich, and I won't live in them cussed
TOM smothery houses. I like the woods, and the river, and hogsheads, and
TOM I'll stick to 'em, too. Blame it all! just as we'd got guns, and a cave,
TOM and all just fixed to rob, here this dern foolishness has got to come up
TOM and spile it all!''
TOM 
TOM Tom saw his opportunity --
TOM 
TOM ``Lookyhere, Huck, being rich ain't going to keep me back from turning
TOM robber.''
TOM 
TOM ``No! Oh, good-licks; are you in real dead-wood earnest, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``Just as dead earnest as I'm sitting here. But Huck, we can't let you
TOM into the gang if you ain't respectable, you know.''
TOM 
TOM Huck's joy was quenched.
TOM 
TOM ``Can't let me in, Tom? Didn't you let me go for a pirate?''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, but that's different. A robber is more high-toned than what a
TOM pirate is -- as a general thing. In most countries they're awful high up
TOM in the nobility -- dukes and such.''
TOM 
TOM ``Now, Tom, hain't you always ben friendly to me? You wouldn't shet me
TOM out, would you, Tom? You wouldn't do that, now, WOULD you, Tom?''
TOM 
TOM ``Huck, I wouldn't want to, and I DON'T want to -- but what would people
TOM say? Why, they'd say, 'Mph! Tom Sawyer's Gang! pretty low characters in
TOM it!' They'd mean you, Huck. You wouldn't like that, and I wouldn't.''
TOM 
TOM Huck was silent for some time, engaged in a mental struggle. Finally he
TOM said:
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I'll go back to the widder for a month and tackle it and see if
TOM I can come to stand it, if you'll let me b'long to the gang, Tom.''
TOM 
TOM ``All right, Huck, it's a whiz! Come along, old chap, and I'll ask the
TOM widow to let up on you a little, Huck.''
TOM 
TOM ``Will you, Tom -- now will you? That's good. If she'll let up on some
TOM of the roughest things, I'll smoke private and cuss private, and crowd
TOM through or bust. When you going to start the gang and turn robbers?''
TOM 
TOM ``Oh, right off. We'll get the boys together and have the initiation
TOM to-night, maybe.''
TOM 
TOM ``Have the which?''
TOM 
TOM ``Have the initiation.''
TOM 
TOM ``What's that?''
TOM 
TOM ``It's to swear to stand by one another, and never tell the gang's
TOM secrets, even if you're chopped all to flinders, and kill anybody and
TOM all his family that hurts one of the gang.''
TOM 
TOM ``That's gay -- that's mighty gay, Tom, I tell you.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, I bet it is. And all that swearing's got to be done at midnight,
TOM in the lonesomest, awfulest place you can find -- a ha'nted house is the
TOM best, but they're all ripped up now.''
TOM 
TOM ``Well, midnight's good, anyway, Tom.''
TOM 
TOM ``Yes, so it is. And you've got to swear on a coffin, and sign it with
TOM blood.''
TOM 
TOM ``Now, that's something LIKE! Why, it's a million times bullier than
TOM pirating. I'll stick to the widder till I rot, Tom; and if I git to be a
TOM reg'lar ripper of a robber, and everybody talking 'bout it, I reckon
TOM she'll be proud she snaked me in out of the wet.''
TOM 
TOM 
TOM CONCLUSION
TOM 
TOM SO endeth this chronicle. It being strictly a history of a BOY, it must
TOM stop here; the story could not go much further without becoming the
TOM history of a MAN. When one writes a novel about grown people, he knows
TOM exactly where to stop -- that is, with a marriage; but when he writes of
TOM juveniles, he must stop where he best can.
TOM 
TOM Most of the characters that perform in this book still live, and are
TOM prosperous and happy. Some day it may seem worth while to take up the
TOM story of the younger ones again and see what sort of men and women they
TOM turned out to be; therefore it will be wisest not to reveal any of that
TOM part of their lives at present.
